<th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color:#ededed">References</th>

<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">I. <span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_talleststatus"><b><a href="#ref_talleststatus">^</a></b></span> <a class="external text" href="https://www.emporis.com/buildings/114095" rel="nofollow">Empire State Building</a> at <i><a href="/wiki/Emporis" title="Emporis">Emporis</a></i><br/>
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-skyscrapercenter_7-8"><a href="#cite_note-skyscrapercenter-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nhlsum_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-nhlsum-12">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nris_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-nris-13">[11]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The <b>Empire State Building</b> is a 102-story<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Floor_count_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Floor_count-10">[b]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Art_Deco" title="Art Deco">Art Deco</a> <a href="/wiki/Early_skyscrapers" title="Early skyscrapers">skyscraper</a> on <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Avenue" title="Fifth Avenue">Fifth Avenue</a> between West <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/33rd_Street_(Manhattan)" title="33rd Street (Manhattan)">33rd</a> and <a href="/wiki/34th_Street_(Manhattan)" title="34th Street (Manhattan)">34th Streets</a> in <a href="/wiki/Midtown_Manhattan" title="Midtown Manhattan">Midtown Manhattan</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>. Designed by <a href="/wiki/Shreve,_Lamb_%26_Harmon" title="Shreve, Lamb &amp; Harmon">Shreve, Lamb &amp; Harmon</a>, the building has a roof height of 1,250 feet (380 m), and with its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antenna_tower" title="Antenna tower">antenna</a> included, it stands a total of 1,454 feet (443.2 m) tall. Its name is derived from "<a href="/wiki/Empire_State" title="Empire State">Empire State</a>", the <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._state_nicknames" title="List of U.S. state nicknames">nickname</a> for <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a>. As of 2017<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Empire_State_Building&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> the Empire State Building is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_buildings_in_the_United_States" title="List of tallest buildings in the United States">fifth-tallest completed skyscraper in the United States</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_buildings_in_the_world" title="List of tallest buildings in the world">28th-tallest in the world</a>. It is also the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_freestanding_structures_in_the_world" title="List of tallest freestanding structures in the world">sixth-tallest freestanding structure in the Americas</a>. When measured by <a href="/wiki/Pinnacle" title="Pinnacle">pinnacle</a> height, it is the fifth-tallest building in the United States.</p>
<p>The site of the Empire State Building was first developed as part of John Thompson's farm in the early 18th century. In the late 1820s, it came into the possession of the prominent <a href="/wiki/Astor_family" title="Astor family">Astor family</a>, with <a href="/wiki/John_Jacob_Astor" title="John Jacob Astor">John Jacob Astor's</a> descendants building the <a href="/wiki/Waldorf%E2%80%93Astoria_(New_York,_1893)" title="Waldorf–Astoria (New York, 1893)">Waldorf–Astoria Hotel</a> on the site in the 1890s. By the 1920s, the family sold the outdated hotel, and the site indirectly ended up under the ownership of Empire State Inc., a business venture that included businessman <a href="/wiki/John_J._Raskob" title="John J. Raskob">John J. Raskob</a> and former New York governor <a href="/wiki/Al_Smith" title="Al Smith">Alfred E. Smith</a>. The Empire State Building, as it was then dubbed, was originally supposed to be a standard 50-story office building. However, the original plans were revised fifteen times, with the tower ultimately being expanded to a 1,250-foot building with 86 stories and an <a href="/wiki/Airship" title="Airship">airship</a> mast on top. This ensured the Empire State Building would be the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_buildings_and_structures_in_the_world" title="List of tallest buildings and structures in the world">world's tallest building</a>, beating out the <a href="/wiki/Chrysler_Building" title="Chrysler Building">Chrysler Building</a> and <a href="/wiki/40_Wall_Street" title="40 Wall Street">40 Wall Street</a>, two other Manhattan skyscrapers under construction at the time that were also vying for that honor.</p>
<p>Demolition of the Waldorf–Astoria began in October 1929, and the foundation of the Empire State Building was excavated before demolition was even complete. Construction on the building itself started on March 17, 1930, with an average construction rate of one floor per day. A well-coordinated schedule meant that the 86 stories were <a href="/wiki/Topping_out" title="Topping out">topped out</a> by September 19 of the same year, with the mast completed by November 21. From that point, interior work proceeded at a quick pace, and the Empire State Building was opened on May 1, 1931, thirteen and a half months after the first steel beam was erected. Despite the publicity surrounding the building's construction, the building's owners failed to make a profit until the early 1950s. However, it has been a popular tourist attraction since opening, with around 4 million visitors to the building's 86th and 102nd floor observatories every year.</p>
<p>The Empire State Building stood as the world's tallest building for nearly 40 years, from its completion until the topping out of the original <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Center_(1973%E2%80%932001)" title="World Trade Center (1973–2001)">World Trade Center</a>'s North Tower in <a href="/wiki/Lower_Manhattan" title="Lower Manhattan">Lower Manhattan</a> in late 1970. Following the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a> in 2001, the Empire State Building was again the tallest building in New York, until <a href="/wiki/One_World_Trade_Center" title="One World Trade Center">One World Trade Center</a> reached a greater height in April 2012.</p>
<p>The Empire State Building is an <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">American</a> <a href="/wiki/Cultural_icon" title="Cultural icon">cultural icon</a>, having been featured in dozens of TV shows and movies since <i><a href="/wiki/King_Kong_(1933_film)" title="King Kong (1933 film)">King Kong</a></i> was released in 1933. A symbol of New York City, it has been named as one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seven_Wonders_of_the_Modern_World" title="Seven Wonders of the Modern World">Seven Wonders of the Modern World</a> by the <a href="/wiki/American_Society_of_Civil_Engineers" title="American Society of Civil Engineers">American Society of Civil Engineers</a>. The building and its street floor interior are designated landmarks of the <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Landmarks_Preservation_Commission" title="New York City Landmarks Preservation Commission">New York City Landmarks Preservation Commission</a>, and confirmed by the <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Board_of_Estimate" title="New York City Board of Estimate">New York City Board of Estimate</a>. It was designated as a <a href="/wiki/National_Historic_Landmark" title="National Historic Landmark">National Historic Landmark</a> in 1986. In 2007, it was ranked number one on the AIA's List of <a href="/wiki/America%27s_Favorite_Architecture" title="America's Favorite Architecture">America's Favorite Architecture</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Location">Location</span></h2>
<p>The Empire State Building is located at 350 Fifth Avenue in <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a>, on the street's west side between 33rd and 34th Streets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackson2010414_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackson2010414-16">[14]</a></sup> Although physically located in South Midtown,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tarquinio_2009_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tarquinio_2009-17">[15]</a></sup> a mixed residential and commercial area,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[16]</a></sup> the building is so large that it uses its own ZIP code of 10118,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bi-facts_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-bi-facts-19">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amny-facts_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-amny-facts-20">[18]</a></sup> being one of 43 buildings in New York City that has its own zip code.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EmpireZip_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-EmpireZip-1">[a]</a></sup> The area to the south and west features other major Manhattan landmarks as well, including <a href="/wiki/Macy%27s_Herald_Square" title="Macy's Herald Square">Macy's</a> at <a href="/wiki/Herald_Square" title="Herald Square">Herald Square</a> on Sixth Avenue and 34th Street,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MTA-PennStation-2015_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-MTA-PennStation-2015-24">[22]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Koreatown,_Manhattan" title="Koreatown, Manhattan">Koreatown</a> on 32nd Street between Fifth and Sixth Avenues,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MTA-PennStation-2015_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-MTA-PennStation-2015-24">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[23]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Station_(New_York_City)" title="Pennsylvania Station (New York City)">Penn Station</a> and <a href="/wiki/Madison_Square_Garden" title="Madison Square Garden">Madison Square Garden</a> on Seventh Avenue between 32nd and 34th Streets,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MTA-PennStation-2015_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-MTA-PennStation-2015-24">[22]</a></sup> and the Flower District on 28th Street between Sixth and Seventh Avenues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[24]</a></sup> The nearest <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Subway" title="New York City Subway">New York City Subway</a> stations are <a href="/wiki/34th_Street%E2%80%93Herald_Square_(New_York_City_Subway)" title="34th Street–Herald Square (New York City Subway)">34th Street–Herald Square</a> at Sixth Avenue and Broadway, one block west, and <a href="/wiki/33rd_Street_(IRT_Lexington_Avenue_Line)" title="33rd Street (IRT Lexington Avenue Line)">33rd Street</a> at Park Avenue, two blocks east. There is also a <a href="/wiki/PATH_(rail_system)" title="PATH (rail system)">PATH</a> station at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/33rd_Street_(PATH_station)" title="33rd Street (PATH station)">33rd Street</a> and Sixth Avenue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MTA-MurrayHill-2015_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-MTA-MurrayHill-2015-27">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>To the east of the Empire State Building is <a href="/wiki/Murray_Hill,_Manhattan" title="Murray Hill, Manhattan">Murray Hill</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MTA-MurrayHill-2015_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-MTA-MurrayHill-2015-27">[25]</a></sup> a mixed-use neighborhood with residential, commercial, and entertainment uses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackson2010866_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackson2010866-28">[26]</a></sup> One block east of the Empire State Building, on Madison Avenue at 34th Street, is the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Public_Library" title="New York Public Library">New York Public Library</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Science,_Industry_and_Business_Library" title="Science, Industry and Business Library">Science, Industry and Business Library</a>, which is located on the same block as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CUNY_Graduate_Center" title="CUNY Graduate Center">CUNY Graduate Center</a>. The library also has its <a href="/wiki/New_York_Public_Library_Main_Branch" title="New York Public Library Main Branch">main and Mid-Manhattan branches</a> at Fifth Avenue and 40th Street, six blocks north.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MTA-MurrayHill-2015_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-MTA-MurrayHill-2015-27">[25]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Library_%26_Museum" title="Morgan Library &amp; Museum">Morgan Library &amp; Museum</a> is located four blocks away, at Madison Avenue and 36th Street.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackson2010866_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackson2010866-28">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MTA-MurrayHill-2015_27-3"><a href="#cite_note-MTA-MurrayHill-2015-27">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Site">Site</span></h3>
<p>The earliest recorded major action on the site was during the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[27]</a></sup> According to one account, shots rang out on what is now Fifth Avenue from "the battle of the cornfield". Some 3,500 of <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a>'s troops were in the city and in danger of being trapped by the <a href="/wiki/British_Army_during_the_American_Revolutionary_War" title="British Army during the American Revolutionary War">British Army</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCarthy_and_Rutherford19316.2C_7_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCarthy_and_Rutherford19316.2C_7-30">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19310502_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19310502-31">[29]</a></sup> At the time, Mary and John Murray owned a farm on <a href="/wiki/Murray_Hill,_Manhattan" title="Murray Hill, Manhattan">Murray Hill</a>, within the area from present-day Madison Avenue to <a href="/wiki/Lexington_Avenue" title="Lexington Avenue">Lexington Avenue</a> between 34th and 38th streets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Craven2009_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Craven2009-32">[30]</a></sup> Mary invited the British officers into her home for food and drink, while <a href="/wiki/Israel_Putnam" title="Israel Putnam">General Putnam</a> was able to lead the 3,500 men out of the city and into <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Harlem_Heights" title="Battle of Harlem Heights">Harlem Heights</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCarthy_and_Rutherford19316.2C_7_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCarthy_and_Rutherford19316.2C_7-30">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1799, John Thompson (or Thomson; accounts vary<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19310502_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19310502-31">[29]</a></sup>) bought a 20-acre (8 ha) tract of land roughly bounded by <a href="/wiki/Madison_Avenue" title="Madison Avenue">Madison Avenue</a>, 36th Street, <a href="/wiki/Sixth_Avenue_(Manhattan)" title="Sixth Avenue (Manhattan)">Sixth Avenue</a>, and 33rd Street, immediately north of the <a href="/wiki/Caspar_Samler_farm" title="Caspar Samler farm">Caspar Samler farm</a>, for 482 <a href="/wiki/Pound_sterling" title="Pound sterling">British pounds</a> (roughly US$2400 at the time).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCarthy_and_Rutherford19315_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCarthy_and_Rutherford19315-33">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[c]</a></sup> At the time, a stream called <i>t'Oude Wrack</i> (Dutch for "Old Wreck") ran across the site, emptying into Sunfish Pond, located at the present-day Park Avenue South and 31st Street in <a href="/wiki/Kips_Bay,_Manhattan" title="Kips Bay, Manhattan">Kips Bay</a>. The stream originated at what is now Broadway and 44th Street in the middle of <a href="/wiki/Times_Square" title="Times Square">Times Square</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kadinsky2016_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kadinsky2016-37">[34]</a></sup> A glue factory polluted Sunfish Pond heavily during the 1820s, and it was infilled in 1839 after most of its water was used to extinguish a fire at the <a href="/wiki/New_York_House_of_Refuge" title="New York House of Refuge">New York House of Refuge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kadinsky2016_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kadinsky2016-37">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thompson was said to have sold the farm to Charles Lawton for $10,000 on September 24, 1825,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperling2003-p124_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sperling2003-p124-35">[33]</a></sup> although details of this sale are unclear, as details of the deed that certified the sale were later lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19310502_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19310502-31">[29]</a></sup> In 1826, <a href="/wiki/John_Jacob_Astor" title="John Jacob Astor">John Jacob Astor</a> of the prominent <a href="/wiki/Astor_family" title="Astor family">Astor family</a> bought the land from Lawton for $20,500.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Craven2009_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Craven2009-32">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[d]</a></sup> The Astors also purchased a parcel from the Murrays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Craven2009_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-Craven2009-32">[30]</a></sup> John Jacob's son <a href="/wiki/William_Backhouse_Astor_Sr." title="William Backhouse Astor Sr.">William Backhouse Astor Sr.</a> bought a half interest on July 28, 1827, for $20,500,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timeline_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-timeline-34">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nps-nom_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-nps-nom-42">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperling2003-p124_35-3"><a href="#cite_note-Sperling2003-p124-35">[33]</a></sup> for a tract of land on Fifth Avenue from 32nd to 35th streets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199120_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199120-40">[37]</a></sup> The Astors had intended to simply reserve the land for possible future development, but the deadly <a href="/wiki/Astor_Place_Riot" title="Astor Place Riot">Astor Place Riot</a> in 1849 forced the Astor family to flee to their Midtown tracts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDouglas2004108_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDouglas2004108-43">[39]</a></sup> William built an unpretentious square red brick house on the southwest corner of 34th Street and Fifth Avenue in 1859, while his brother <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Jacob_Astor_II" title="John Jacob Astor II">John Jacob Astor Jr.</a> erected a home at the northwest corner of 33rd Street in 1862.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timeline_34-3"><a href="#cite_note-timeline-34">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199120_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199120-40">[37]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Waldorf_Hotel_1893.jpg"><img alt="The Waldorf Hotel, which stood on the Empire State Building site from 1893 to 1929" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1488" data-file-width="950" height="266" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Waldorf_Hotel_1893.jpg/170px-Waldorf_Hotel_1893.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Waldorf_Hotel_1893.jpg/255px-Waldorf_Hotel_1893.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Waldorf_Hotel_1893.jpg/340px-Waldorf_Hotel_1893.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Waldorf_Hotel_1893.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Waldorf%E2%80%93Astoria_(New_York,_1893)" title="Waldorf–Astoria (New York, 1893)">Waldorf Hotel</a> (1893), which stood on the site of the Empire State Building until 1929</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1890, <a href="/wiki/William_Waldorf_Astor" title="William Waldorf Astor">William Waldorf Astor</a>, the grandson of John Sr. and son of John Jr., inherited his father's mansion at the corner of Fifth Avenue and 33rd Street, and started building the <a href="/wiki/Waldorf%E2%80%93Astoria_(New_York,_1893)" title="Waldorf–Astoria (New York, 1893)">Waldorf Hotel</a> on the site. This decision originated in part from a dispute with his aunt <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caroline_Webster_Schermerhorn_Astor" title="Caroline Webster Schermerhorn Astor">Caroline Webster Schermerhorn Astor</a>, the wife of <a href="/wiki/John_Jacob_Astor_III" title="John Jacob Astor III">John Jacob Astor III</a> who lived next door, and whether Caroline or Mary Dahlgren Paul Astor, William's wife, would be known in society as "the" Mrs. Astor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDouglas2004108_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDouglas2004108-43">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014113_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014113-44">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[41]</a></sup> The planned hotel was supposed to be an 11-story building, as per the specifications of founding proprietor <a href="/wiki/George_Boldt" title="George Boldt">George Boldt</a>, but his wife Louise believed that 13 was a lucky number and persuaded her husband to add two floors to the construction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCarthy_and_Rutherford193131-32_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCarthy_and_Rutherford193131-32-46">[42]</a></sup> William Astor's construction of a hotel next to his aunt's house caused both Astors to threaten to demolish their home and build a stable on the property,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[43]</a></sup> but with Boldt's assistance, John Astor persuaded his mother to move uptown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[44]</a></sup> The Waldorf Hotel, named after the Astors' ancestral home in the little German town of <a href="/wiki/Walldorf" title="Walldorf">Walldorf</a>, was opened for business March 13, 1893.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDouglas2004108_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDouglas2004108-43">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCarthy_and_Rutherford193123_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCarthy_and_Rutherford193123-49">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>When a decision was made to build a second hotel next to the Waldorf, truce provisions were developed between the Astors, including the use of corridors between the buildings that could be bricked up if needed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014114_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014114-50">[46]</a></sup> On November 1, 1897, Waldorf's cousin, <a href="/wiki/John_Jacob_Astor_IV" title="John Jacob Astor IV">John Jacob Astor IV</a>, opened the 16-story Astoria Hotel on an adjacent site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-history-51">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDouglas2004108_43-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDouglas2004108-43">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCarthy_and_Rutherford193177_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCarthy_and_Rutherford193177-52">[48]</a></sup> The Astoria, named after the Oregon town of <a href="/wiki/Astoria,_Oregon" title="Astoria, Oregon">Astoria</a> that had been founded by John Sr. in 1811, stood on the site of William Sr's house, and was leased to Boldt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBiggs189731_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBiggs189731-53">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-history-51">[47]</a></sup> The two hotels, under one management, were renamed the Waldorf–Astoria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBiggs189729_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBiggs189729-54">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[51]</a></sup> The hotel became, according to author Sean Dennis Cashman, "a successful symbol of the opulence and achievement of the Astor family".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[52]</a></sup> The hotel was frequented by <a href="/wiki/Ward_McAllister#.22The_Four_Hundred.22" title="Ward McAllister">The Four Hundred</a>, the social elite of New York.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nps-nom_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-nps-nom-42">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>The combined hotel had 1,300 bedrooms, making it the largest hotel in the world at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[53]</a></sup> Of these, the Astoria portion contained 25 public rooms and 550 guest rooms,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[54]</a></sup> while the Waldorf portion contained 15 public rooms, 450 guest rooms, and 100 rooms for servants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[55]</a></sup> It was designed specifically to cater to the needs of socially prominent "wealthy upper crust" of New York and distinguished foreign visitors to the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III19918_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III19918-60">[56]</a></sup> It was the first hotel to offer complete electricity and private bathrooms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[58]</a></sup> The Waldorf gained world renown for its fundraising dinners and balls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>Boldt died in early 1918, and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Coleman_du_Pont&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Coleman du Pont (page does not exist)">Coleman du Pont</a> bought the hotel's lease soon after Boldt's death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014117_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014117-64">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[61]</a></sup> By the 1920s, the hotel was becoming dated, and the elegant social life of New York had moved much farther north than 34th Street.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014118_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014118-66">[62]</a></sup> The Astor family decided to build a <a href="/wiki/Waldorf_Astoria_New_York" title="Waldorf Astoria New York">replacement hotel</a> further uptown,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDouglas2004108_43-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDouglas2004108-43">[39]</a></sup> and sold the hotel to the Bethlehem Engineering Corporation in 1928 for $14–16 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014118_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014118-66">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timeline_34-4"><a href="#cite_note-timeline-34">[32]</a></sup> They closed the hotel on May 3, 1929.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-history-51">[47]</a></sup> The Waldorf–Astoria Hotel records of 1893–1929 are held by the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Public_Library" title="New York Public Library">New York Public Library</a>'s Archives &amp; Manuscripts division.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Planning_process">Planning process<span id="Plans"></span></span></h3>
<p>The Bethlehem Engineering Corporation originally wanted to build a 25-story office building on the Waldorf–Astoria's site. The company's president, Floyd De L. Brown, paid $100,000 of the $1 million <a href="/wiki/Down_payment" title="Down payment">down payment</a> required to start construction on the tower, with the promise that the difference would be paid later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDouglas2004108_43-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDouglas2004108-43">[39]</a></sup> Brown borrowed $900,000 from a bank, but then <a href="/wiki/Default_(finance)" title="Default (finance)">defaulted</a> on the loan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDouglas2004109_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDouglas2004109-68">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199885.E2.80.9387_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199885.E2.80.9387-69">[65]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Empire_State_Building_by_David_Shankbone_crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3091" data-file-width="1706" height="308" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Empire_State_Building_by_David_Shankbone_crop.jpg/170px-Empire_State_Building_by_David_Shankbone_crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Empire_State_Building_by_David_Shankbone_crop.jpg/255px-Empire_State_Building_by_David_Shankbone_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Empire_State_Building_by_David_Shankbone_crop.jpg/340px-Empire_State_Building_by_David_Shankbone_crop.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Empire_State_Building_by_David_Shankbone_crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The building plans went through fifteen versions before the current plan was approved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200981_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200981-70">[66]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The land was then resold to Empire State Inc., a group of wealthy investors that included <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louis_G._Kaufman" title="Louis G. Kaufman">Louis G. Kaufman</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ellis_P._Earle&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ellis P. Earle (page does not exist)">Ellis P. Earle</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_J._Raskob" title="John J. Raskob">John J. Raskob</a>, Coleman du Pont, and <a href="/wiki/Pierre_S._du_Pont" title="Pierre S. du Pont">Pierre S. du Pont</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDouglas2004109_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDouglas2004109-68">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199885.E2.80.9387_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199885.E2.80.9387-69">[65]</a></sup> The name came from the <a href="/wiki/Empire_State" title="Empire State">state nickname</a> for New York.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAl-Kodmany201772_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAl-Kodmany201772-71">[67]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alfred_E._Smith" title="Alfred E. Smith">Alfred E. Smith</a>, a former <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_New_York" title="Governor of New York">Governor of New York</a> and a former U.S. presidential candidate whose <a href="/wiki/Al_Smith_presidential_campaign,_1928" title="Al Smith presidential campaign, 1928">1928 campaign</a> had been managed by Raskob,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200979_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200979-72">[68]</a></sup> was appointed as the head of the company.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDouglas2004109_68-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDouglas2004109-68">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199885.E2.80.9387_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199885.E2.80.9387-69">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timeline_34-5"><a href="#cite_note-timeline-34">[32]</a></sup> The Empire State Inc. consortium became public in August 1929.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200980_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200980-73">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19290805_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19290805-74">[70]</a></sup> The group also purchased nearby land plots so they would have the 2 acres (1 ha) needed for the tower's base, with the combined plot measuring 425 feet (130 m) wide by 200 feet (61 m) long.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19290805_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19290805-74">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>Empire State Inc. hired <a href="/wiki/William_F._Lamb" title="William F. Lamb">William F. Lamb</a>, from the architectural firm <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shreve,_Lamb_and_Harmon" title="Shreve, Lamb and Harmon">Shreve, Lamb and Harmon</a>, to design the building.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackson2010413_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackson2010413-2">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200989_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200989-75">[71]</a></sup> Lamb produced the building drawings in just two weeks, using the firm's earlier designs for the <a href="/wiki/Reynolds_Building" title="Reynolds Building">Reynolds Building</a> in <a href="/wiki/Winston-Salem,_North_Carolina" title="Winston-Salem, North Carolina">Winston-Salem, North Carolina</a>, as a basis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAl-Kodmany201772_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAl-Kodmany201772-71">[67]</a></sup> Meanwhile, Lamb's partner <a href="/wiki/Richmond_Shreve" title="Richmond Shreve">Richmond Shreve</a> created "bug diagrams" of what needed to be done simultaneously, coordinating tasks to the hour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBascomb2004246.E2.80.93247_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBascomb2004246.E2.80.93247-76">[72]</a></sup> Because of the <a href="/wiki/1916_Zoning_Resolution" title="1916 Zoning Resolution">1916 Zoning Act</a>, Lamb could not build a structure with walls that ascended straight up from the street; instead, they were required to incorporate <a href="/wiki/Setback_(architecture)" title="Setback (architecture)">setbacks</a>, making the lower floors much larger than the upper floors. As a result, the tower was designed from the top down,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWagner200312_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWagner200312-77">[73]</a></sup> which gave it a "pencil"-like shape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014157_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014157-78">[74]</a></sup> During the design process, Raskob put a large pencil on a table and asked Lamb, "How high could you make it so it won't fall down?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200981_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200981-70">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dupre_2013_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dupre_2013-79">[75]</a></sup> The original plan for the building was slated to be 50 stories,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200981_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200981-70">[66]</a></sup> but the height was later increased to 60 and then 80 stories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19290805_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19290805-74">[70]</a></sup> The planned 80-story building,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199814_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199814-80">[76]</a></sup> with a height of about 1,000 feet (300 m),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014129_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014129-81">[77]</a></sup> would have height limits on nearby buildings ensuring that the top fifty floors would get expansive views of the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19290805_74-3"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19290805-74">[70]</a></sup> <i>The New York Times</i> lauded the site's proximity to transportation, with the <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn%E2%80%93Manhattan_Transit_Corporation" title="Brooklyn–Manhattan Transit Corporation">Brooklyn–Manhattan Transit's</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/34th_Street%E2%80%93Herald_Square_(BMT_Broadway_Line)" title="34th Street–Herald Square (BMT Broadway Line)">34th Street</a> station and the <a href="/wiki/PATH_(rail_system)" title="PATH (rail system)">Hudson and Manhattan Railroad's</a> <a href="/wiki/33rd_Street_station_(PATH)" title="33rd Street station (PATH)">33rd Street</a> terminal one block away, as well as <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Station_(1910%E2%80%931963)" title="Pennsylvania Station (1910–1963)">Penn Station</a> two blocks away and the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Central_Terminal" title="Grand Central Terminal">Grand Central Terminal</a> nine blocks away at its closest. It also praised the 3,000,000 square feet (280,000 m<sup>2</sup>) of proposed floor space near "one of the busiest sections in the world".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19290805_74-4"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19290805-74">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>While plans for the Empire State Building were being finalized, there was an intense competition in New York for the title of "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_buildings_in_the_world" title="List of tallest buildings in the world">world's tallest building</a>", and the <a href="/wiki/40_Wall_Street" title="40 Wall Street">40 Wall Street</a> (then the Bank of Manhattan Building) and the <a href="/wiki/Chrysler_Building" title="Chrysler Building">Chrysler Building</a> in Manhattan were both under construction when work began on the Empire State Building.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199814_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199814-80">[76]</a></sup> The "Race into the Sky", as popular media called it at the time, was representative of the country's optimism in the 1920s, fueled by the building boom in major cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERasenberger2009388.E2.80.93389_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERasenberger2009388.E2.80.93389-82">[78]</a></sup> The 40 Wall Street tower was revised from 840 feet (260 m) to 925 feet (282 m) in April 1929, making it the world's tallest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014130_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014130-83">[79]</a></sup> The Chrysler Building added its 185-foot (56 m) steel tip to its roof in October 1929, bringing it to a height of 1,046 feet (319 m) and greatly exceeding 40 Wall Street's height.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199814_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199814-80">[76]</a></sup> The Chrysler Building's developer, <a href="/wiki/Walter_Chrysler" title="Walter Chrysler">Walter Chrysler</a>, realized that his tower's height would exceed the Empire State Building's as well, having ordered his architect <a href="/wiki/William_Van_Alen" title="William Van Alen">William Van Alen</a> to change the Chrysler's original roof from a stubby <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_architecture" title="Romanesque architecture">Romanesque</a> dome to the narrow steel spire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014130_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014130-83">[79]</a></sup> Raskob, wishing to have the Empire State Building be the world's tallest, reviewed the plans and realized that he could add five more floors and a spire of his own; however, the new floors would need to be set back because of projected wind pressure on the extension.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014131_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014131-84">[80]</a></sup> On November 18, 1929, Smith acquired a lot at 27–31 West 33rd Street, adding 75 feet (23 m) to the width of the proposed office building's site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBascomb2004230_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBascomb2004230-85">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[82]</a></sup> Two days later, Smith announced the updated plans for the skyscraper, with an observation deck on the 86th-floor roof at a height of 1,050 feet (320 m), higher than the Chrysler's 71st-floor observation deck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014131_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014131-84">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 1,050-foot Empire State Building would only be 4 feet (1.2 m) taller than the Chrysler Building,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014131_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014131-84">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldman198031.E2.80.9332_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldman198031.E2.80.9332-87">[83]</a></sup> and Raskob was afraid that Chrysler might try to "pull a trick like hiding a rod in the spire and then sticking it up at the last minute."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200981_70-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200981-70">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBascomb2004235_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBascomb2004235-88">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldman198031.E2.80.9332_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldman198031.E2.80.9332-87">[83]</a></sup> The plans were revised one last time in December 1929, with a 16-story, 200-foot (61 m) metal "crown" and an additional 222-foot (68 m) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dirigible" title="Dirigible">dirigible</a> mooring mast. The roof height was now 1,250 feet (380 m), making it the tallest building in the world by far, even without the antenna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBascomb2004247_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBascomb2004247-89">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200981_70-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200981-70">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014185_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014185-90">[86]</a></sup> The addition of the dirigible station meant that another floor, the now-enclosed 86th floor, would have to be built below the crown;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014185_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014185-90">[86]</a></sup> however, unlike the Chrysler's spire, the Empire State's mast would serve a practical purpose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBascomb2004235_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBascomb2004235-88">[84]</a></sup> By this time the blueprints for the building had gone through up to fifteen versions before they were approved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200981_70-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200981-70">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dupre_2013_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dupre_2013-79">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Observer-ESB-Challenged-1976_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Observer-ESB-Challenged-1976-91">[87]</a></sup> Lamb described the other specifications he was given for the final, approved plan:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The program was short enough—a fixed budget, no space more than 28 feet from window to corridor, as many stories of such space as possible, an exterior of limestone, and completion date of [May 1], 1931, which meant a year and six months from the beginning of sketches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWillis199595_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillis199595-92">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200981_70-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200981-70">[66]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The general contractors were named as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Starrett_Brothers_and_Eken" title="Starrett Brothers and Eken">Starrett Brothers and Eken</a>, composed of <a href="/wiki/Paul_Starrett" title="Paul Starrett">Paul</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_A._Starrett" title="William A. Starrett">William A. Starrett</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Andrew_J._Eken&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Andrew J. Eken (page does not exist)">Andrew J. Eken</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200990_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200990-93">[89]</a></sup> who also constructed other New York City buildings and developments such as the original <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stuyvesant_Town" title="Stuyvesant Town">Stuyvesant Town</a>, <a href="/wiki/Starrett_City,_Brooklyn" title="Starrett City, Brooklyn">Starrett City</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trump_Tower" title="Trump Tower">Trump Tower</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackson20101230_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackson20101230-94">[90]</a></sup> The project was financed primarily by Raskob and Pierre du Pont,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlowers200117_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlowers200117-95">[91]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/James_Farley" title="James Farley">James Farley</a>'s General Builders Supply Corporation supplied the building materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackson2010413_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackson2010413-2">[1]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_W._Bowser" title="John W. Bowser">John W. Bowser</a> was the project's construction superintendent,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199846_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199846-96">[92]</a></sup> and the structural engineer of the building was Homer G. Balcom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200989_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200989-75">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-BalcomDead-1938_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-BalcomDead-1938-97">[93]</a></sup> The plans were worked out such that construction started on one task before plans for others were finalized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200984_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200984-98">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Construction">Construction</span></h3>
<p>Demolition of the old Waldorf–Astoria began on October 1, 1929.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[95]</a></sup> Stripping the building's materials was an arduous process, as the hotel was made of stiffer material than earlier buildings had, and the granite, precious metals, and wood chips used in the old hotel were not in high demand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014199.26ndash.3B200_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014199.26ndash.3B200-100">[96]</a></sup> Most of the wood eventually either went into a woodpile on nearby 30th Street or was burned in a swamp elsewhere. A lot of the other materials that made up the old hotel, including the granite and bronze, were dumped into the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a> off the shore of <a href="/wiki/Sandy_Hook" title="Sandy Hook">Sandy Hook</a>, New Jersey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014200_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014200-101">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDouglas2004111_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDouglas2004111-102">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the time the hotel's demolition started, Raskob had secured all of the investors that he needed to fund the construction of the building.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014268_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014268-103">[99]</a></sup> The plan was to start construction later that year, but on October 24, the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Stock_Exchange" title="New York Stock Exchange">New York Stock Exchange</a> started a <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_Crash_of_1929" title="Wall Street Crash of 1929">steep and sudden crash</a>, sparking what would become the decade-long <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>. Even so, Raskob refused to cancel the project because of the progress that had been made up to that point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200980_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200980-73">[69]</a></sup> Neither Raskob nor Smith lost much money in the crash: Raskob because he had stopped his <a href="/wiki/Speculation" title="Speculation">speculation</a> about the stock market the previous year, Smith because he had never invested a great amount of money in the market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014268_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014268-103">[99]</a></sup> However, most of their investors did lose money, and as a result, in December of that year, the <a href="/wiki/MetLife" title="MetLife">Metropolitan Life Insurance Company</a> loaned Empire State Inc. some $27.5 million so the group could start construction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[100]</a></sup> Even though the stock market crash obviated the need for any new office space, Raskob and Smith proceeded to start construction anyway,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERasenberger2009394.E2.80.93395_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERasenberger2009394.E2.80.93395-105">[101]</a></sup> since canceling the project would cause investors to incur great losses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200980_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200980-73">[69]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Old_timer_structural_worker2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2401" data-file-width="3000" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Old_timer_structural_worker2.jpg/220px-Old_timer_structural_worker2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Old_timer_structural_worker2.jpg/330px-Old_timer_structural_worker2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Old_timer_structural_worker2.jpg/440px-Old_timer_structural_worker2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Old_timer_structural_worker2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A worker bolts beams during construction; the <a href="/wiki/Chrysler_Building" title="Chrysler Building">Chrysler Building</a> can be seen in the background.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A structural steel contract was awarded on January 12, 1930,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[102]</a></sup> and excavation of the site began ten days later on January 22,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200985_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200985-107">[103]</a></sup> before the old hotel had been completely demolished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014203_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014203-108">[104]</a></sup> Two twelve-hour shifts, consisting of 300 men each, worked continuously to dig the 55-foot (17 m) foundation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200985_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200985-107">[103]</a></sup> Small pier holes were sunk into the ground to house the concrete footings that would support the steelwork.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014205_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014205-109">[105]</a></sup> Excavation was nearly complete by early March,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[106]</a></sup> and construction on the building itself started on March 17,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014207_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014207-111">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackson2010413_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackson2010413-2">[1]</a></sup> with the builders placing the first steel columns on the completed footings before the rest of the footings had been finished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014206_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014206-112">[108]</a></sup> Around this time, Lamb held a press conference on the building plans. He described the reflective steel panels parallel to the windows, the large-block <a href="/wiki/Indiana_Limestone" title="Indiana Limestone">Indiana Limestone</a> facade that was slightly more expensive than smaller bricks, and the tower's lines and rise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBascomb2004247_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBascomb2004247-89">[85]</a></sup> Four colossal columns, intended for installation in the center of the building site, were delivered; they would support a combined 10,000,000 pounds (4,500,000 kg) when the building was finished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBascomb2004248_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBascomb2004248-113">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>The building's structural steel was pre-ordered and <a href="/wiki/Prefabrication" title="Prefabrication">pre-fabricated</a> in anticipation of a revision to the city's building code that would have allowed the Empire State Building's structural steel to carry 18,000 pounds per square inch (124,106 kPa), up from 16,000 pounds per square inch (110,316 kPa), thus reducing the amount of steel needed for the building. Although the 18,000-psi regulation had been safely enacted in other cities, Mayor <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Walker" title="Jimmy Walker">Jimmy Walker</a> did not sign the new codes into law until March 26, 1930, just before the steel was about to be laid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014207_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014207-111">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[110]</a></sup> The first steel framework was put into place on April 1, 1930.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[111]</a></sup> From there, construction proceeded at a fast pace. During one stretch of 10 working days, the builders erected fourteen floors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014204_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014204-116">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackson2010413_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackson2010413-2">[1]</a></sup> This was made possible due to the extremely precise coordination of the building's planning, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Mass_production" title="Mass production">mass production</a> of common materials such as windows and <a href="/wiki/Spandrel" title="Spandrel">spandrels</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014204.E2.80.93205_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014204.E2.80.93205-117">[113]</a></sup> For instance, after a supplier of dark Hauteville marble could not deliver enough material on time, Starrett switched to using Rose Famosa marble from a German quarry that was purchased specifically to provide the project with sufficient marble.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014205_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014205-109">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>The scale of the project was massive, with trucks carrying "16,000 partition tiles, 5,000 bags of cement, 450 cubic yards [340 m<sup>3</sup>] of sand and 300 bags of lime" arriving at the construction site every day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19300727_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19300727-118">[114]</a></sup> There were also cafes and concession stands on five of the incomplete floors so workers did not have to descend to the ground level to eat lunch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200986_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200986-3">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014222_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014222-119">[115]</a></sup> Temporary water taps were also built so that workers did not waste time buying water bottles from the ground level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200986_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200986-3">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014223_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014223-120">[116]</a></sup> Additionally, small railway systems carried materials from the basement storage spaces<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200986_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200986-3">[2]</a></sup> to elevators that brought the carts to the desired floors, where the carts then delivered the materials across that level directly by using another set of tracks on that floor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19300727_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19300727-118">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPopular_MechanicsDecember_1930922_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPopular_MechanicsDecember_1930922-121">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014222_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014222-119">[115]</a></sup> The 57,480 short tons (51,320 long tons) of steel ordered for the project was the largest-ever single order of steel at the time, comprising more steel than was ordered for the Chrysler Building and 40 Wall Street combined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBascomb2004246_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBascomb2004246-122">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERasenberger2009396_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERasenberger2009396-123">[119]</a></sup> According to historian <a href="/wiki/John_Tauranac" title="John Tauranac">John Tauranac</a>, the materials for the building came from far and wide, with "limestone from Indiana, steel girders from Pittsburgh, cement and mortar from upper New York State, marble from Italy, France, and England, wood from northern and Pacific Coast forests, [and] hardware from New England."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014204_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014204-116">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>By June 20, the steel structure had risen to the 26th floor, and by July 27, half of the steel skeleton had been completed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19300727_118-2"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19300727-118">[114]</a></sup> Starrett Bros. and Eken endeavored to build one floor a day in order to speed up construction, a goal that they almost reached with their pace of <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap">4<span class="visualhide"> </span><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>2</sub></span> stories per week; prior to this, the fastest pace of construction for a building of similar height had been <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap">3<span class="visualhide"> </span><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>2</sub></span> stories per week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014212_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014212-124">[120]</a></sup> In the meantime, the final designs for the floors were being designed from the ground up (as opposed to the general design, which had been from the roof down). Some of the levels were still undergoing final approval, with several orders placed within an hour of a plan's being finalized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014212_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014212-124">[120]</a></sup> On September 10, as steelwork was nearing completion, Smith laid the building's <a href="/wiki/Cornerstone" title="Cornerstone">cornerstone</a> during a ceremony attended by thousands. The stone contained a box with contemporary artifacts such as the previous day's <i>New York Times</i>; a U.S. currency set containing all denominations of notes and coins minted in 1930; a history of the site and building; and photographs of the people involved in construction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014213_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014213-126">[122]</a></sup> The steel structure was topped out at 1,048 feet (319 m) on September 19, twelve days ahead of schedule and 23 weeks after the start of construction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman1998164_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman1998164-127">[123]</a></sup> Workers raised a flag atop the 86th floor to signify this milestone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014212_124-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014212-124">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19300920_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19300920-128">[124]</a></sup></p>
<p>Afterward, work on the building's interior and crowning mast commenced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19300920_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19300920-128">[124]</a></sup> The mooring mast topped out on November 21, two months after the steelwork had been completed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014213_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014213-126">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[125]</a></sup> Meanwhile, work on the walls and interior was progressing at a quick pace, with exterior walls built up to the 75th floor by the time steelwork had been built to the 95th floor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014214_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014214-130">[126]</a></sup> The majority of the facade was already finished by the middle of November.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200986_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200986-3">[2]</a></sup> Because of the building's height, it was deemed infeasible to have many elevators or large elevator cabins, so the builders contracted with the <a href="/wiki/Otis_Elevator_Company" title="Otis Elevator Company">Otis Elevator Company</a> to make 66 cars that could speed at 1,200 feet per minute (366 m/min), which represented the largest-ever elevator order at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014215_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014215-131">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to the time constraint builders had, there were also space limitations because construction materials had to be delivered quickly, and trucks needed to drop off these materials without congesting traffic. This was solved by creating a temporary driveway for the trucks between 33rd and 34th Streets, and then storing the materials in the building's first floor and basements. <a href="/wiki/Concrete_mixer" title="Concrete mixer">Concrete mixers</a>, brick hoppers, and stone hoists inside the building ensured that materials would be able to ascend quickly and without endangering or inconveniencing the public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014214_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014214-130">[126]</a></sup> At one point, over 200 trucks made material deliveries at the building site every day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200986_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200986-3">[2]</a></sup> A series of relay and erection <a href="/wiki/Derrick" title="Derrick">derricks</a>, placed on platforms erected near the building, lifted the steel from the trucks below and installed the beams at the appropriate locations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014208_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014208-132">[128]</a></sup> The Empire State Building was structurally completed on April 11, 1931, twelve days ahead of schedule and 410 days after construction commenced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200986_3-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200986-3">[2]</a></sup> Al Smith shot the final rivet, which was made of solid gold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014213.E2.80.93214_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014213.E2.80.93214-133">[129]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Icarus,_Empire_State_Building_MET_DP106525.jpg"><img alt="A photograph of a cable worker, taken by Lewis Hine as part of his project to document the Empire State Building's construction" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2760" data-file-width="3519" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Icarus%2C_Empire_State_Building_MET_DP106525.jpg/220px-Icarus%2C_Empire_State_Building_MET_DP106525.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Icarus%2C_Empire_State_Building_MET_DP106525.jpg/330px-Icarus%2C_Empire_State_Building_MET_DP106525.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Icarus%2C_Empire_State_Building_MET_DP106525.jpg/440px-Icarus%2C_Empire_State_Building_MET_DP106525.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Icarus,_Empire_State_Building_MET_DP106525.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Photograph of a cable worker taken by <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Hine" title="Lewis Hine">Lewis Hine</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The project involved more than 3,500 workers at its peak,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackson2010413_2-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackson2010413-2">[1]</a></sup> including 3,439 on a single day, August 14, 1930.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman1998130.E2.80.93133_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman1998130.E2.80.93133-134">[130]</a></sup> Many of the workers were Irish and Italian immigrants,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman1998204_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman1998204-135">[131]</a></sup> with a sizable minority of <a href="/wiki/Mohawk_people" title="Mohawk people">Mohawk</a> <a href="/wiki/Ironworker" title="Ironworker">ironworkers</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Kahnawake" title="Kahnawake">Kahnawake</a> reserve near <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">Montreal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman1998204_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman1998204-135">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERasenberger2009390.E2.80.93391_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERasenberger2009390.E2.80.93391-136">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[133]</a></sup> According to official accounts, five workers died during the construction,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERasenberger2009398_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERasenberger2009398-138">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014221_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014221-139">[135]</a></sup> although the <i>New York Daily News</i> gave reports of 14 deaths<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200986_3-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200986-3">[2]</a></sup> and a headline in the socialist magazine <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Masses" title="The New Masses">The New Masses</a></i> spread unfounded rumors of up to 42 deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlowers200172_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlowers200172-140">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014221_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014221-139">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lewis_Hine" title="Lewis Hine">Lewis Hine</a> took a myriad of photographs providing not only a documentation of the construction, but also a glimpse into common day life of workers in that era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200985_107-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200985-107">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014283_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014283-142">[138]</a></sup> Hine's images provided much fodder for the media, with many news sources publishing daily press releases that featured one of Hine's pictures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014283.E2.80.93284_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014283.E2.80.93284-143">[139]</a></sup> According to Jim Rasenberger, Hine "climbed out onto the steel with the ironworkers and dangled from a derrick cable hundreds of feet above the city to capture, as no one ever had before (or has since), the dizzy work of building skyscrapers". In Rasenberger's words, Hine turned what might have been an assignment of "corporate flak" into "exhilarating art".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERasenberger2009398.E2.80.93399_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERasenberger2009398.E2.80.93399-144">[140]</a></sup> These images were later organized into their own collection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young_Young_2007_p._144_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Young_Young_2007_p._144-145">[141]</a></sup> The steelworkers' sheer height enraptured onlookers, with <i>New York</i> magazine writing of the steelworkers: "Like little spiders they toiled, spinning a fabric of steel against the sky".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014208_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014208-132">[128]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Opening_and_early_years">Opening and early years</span></h3>
<p>The Empire State Building was officially opened on May 1, 1931, in dramatic fashion, when United States President <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">Herbert Hoover</a> turned on the building's lights with the push of a button from <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200987_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200987-146">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014227.E2.80.93228_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014227.E2.80.93228-147">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-Open-1931_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-Open-1931-4">[3]</a></sup> The tower had been completed forty-five days earlier than its projected opening.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackson2010413_2-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackson2010413-2">[1]</a></sup> Jimmy Walker, Governor <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a>, and Al Smith were also in attendance. After the ceremonial button pressing, they and about 350 other guests proceeded to attend a luncheon on the 86th floor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-Open-1931_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-Open-1931-4">[3]</a></sup> An account from that day stated that the view from the luncheon was obscured by a fog, with other landmarks such as the <a href="/wiki/Statue_of_Liberty" title="Statue of Liberty">Statue of Liberty</a> being "lost in the mist".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CortlandStandard-Open-1931_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-CortlandStandard-Open-1931-148">[144]</a></sup> The building officially opened the next day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CortlandStandard-Open-1931_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-CortlandStandard-Open-1931-148">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199846_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199846-96">[92]</a></sup> Advertisements for the observatories were placed in local newspapers, while nearby hotels also released ads that lauded their proximity to the newly opened tower.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014231_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014231-149">[145]</a></sup></p>
<p>Builders and real estate speculators predicted that the 1,250-foot-tall (380 m) Empire State Building would be the world's tallest building "for many years", thus ending the great New York City skyscraper rivalry. At the time, most engineers agreed that it would be much harder to build a building taller than 1,200 feet (370 m), even with the hardy Manhattan <a href="/wiki/Bedrock" title="Bedrock">bedrock</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[146]</a></sup> (Technically, it was possible to build a tower of up to 2,000 feet (610 m), but it was deemed uneconomical to do so, especially given the fact that the Great Depression was ongoing at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPopular_MechanicsDecember_1930922_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPopular_MechanicsDecember_1930922-121">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[147]</a></sup>) As the tallest building in the world and the first one to exceed 100 floors, the Empire State Building became an icon of the city, and ultimately, of the entire country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199856_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199856-152">[148]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Empire_State_Building_pano.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="9000" data-file-width="3865" height="408" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Empire_State_Building_pano.jpg/175px-Empire_State_Building_pano.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Empire_State_Building_pano.jpg/263px-Empire_State_Building_pano.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Empire_State_Building_pano.jpg/350px-Empire_State_Building_pano.jpg 2x" width="175"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Empire_State_Building_pano.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
View of the building from the north</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Empire State Building's opening coincided with the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression_in_the_United_States" title="Great Depression in the United States">Great Depression in the United States</a>, and as a result much of its office space was vacant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young_Young_2007_p._144_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-Young_Young_2007_p._144-145">[141]</a></sup> In the first year, 23% of the available space was rented;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199848_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199848-153">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014273_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014273-154">[150]</a></sup> by contrast, during the 1920s, the average building would be 52% rented upon opening and 90% rented within five years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014271_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014271-155">[151]</a></sup> The lack of renters led New Yorkers to deride the building as the "Empty State Building".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199848_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199848-153">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young_Young_2007_p._144_145-2"><a href="#cite_note-Young_Young_2007_p._144-145">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[152]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jack_Brod" title="Jack Brod">Jack Brod</a>, the building's longest-lasting original tenant,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tribune19940717_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-tribune19940717-157">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ramirez_1996_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ramirez_1996-158">[154]</a></sup> co-established the Empire Diamond Corporation with his father in the building in mid-1931<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt20080113_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt20080113-159">[155]</a></sup> and rented space in the building until he died in 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt20080113_159-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyt20080113-159">[155]</a></sup> Brod later recalled that there were only about 20 tenants at the time of opening, including him,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ramirez_1996_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ramirez_1996-158">[154]</a></sup> and that Al Smith was the only real tenant in the space above his seventh-floor offices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tribune19940717_157-1"><a href="#cite_note-tribune19940717-157">[153]</a></sup> Generally, during the early 1930s, it was rare for an entity to rent more than a single office in the building, despite Smith's and Raskob's aggressive marketing efforts in the newspapers and to anyone they knew.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014273.E2.80.93274.2C_276.2C_279.E2.80.93280_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014273.E2.80.93274.2C_276.2C_279.E2.80.93280-160">[156]</a></sup> The building's lights were continuously left on, even in the unrented spaces, to give the impression of occupancy. This was exacerbated by competition from the <a href="/wiki/Rockefeller_Center" title="Rockefeller Center">Rockefeller Center</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199848_153-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199848-153">[149]</a></sup> as well as from buildings on <a href="/wiki/42nd_Street_(Manhattan)" title="42nd Street (Manhattan)">42nd Street</a>, which, combined with the Empire State Building, provided excessive space in a slow market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014311_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014311-161">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>Aggressive marketing efforts served to reinforce the Empire State Building's status as the world's tallest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014286.E2.80.93289_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014286.E2.80.93289-162">[158]</a></sup> The observatory was advertised in local newspapers as well as on railroad tickets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014290.E2.80.93291_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014290.E2.80.93291-163">[159]</a></sup> The building became a popular tourist attraction, with one million people each paying one dollar to ride elevators to the observation decks in 1931.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackson20101326_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackson20101326-164">[160]</a></sup> In its first year of operation, the observation deck took in approximately 2 million dollars, as much money as its owners made in rent that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199848_153-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199848-153">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young_Young_2007_p._144_145-3"><a href="#cite_note-Young_Young_2007_p._144-145">[141]</a></sup> By 1936, the top of the building was consistently crowded from sunset to midnight, with people heading to the top to eat leisurely $1.25 dinners with a "wide selection of long, chill drinks".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014291_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014291-165">[161]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/NBC" title="NBC">NBC</a> also took advantage of the building, leasing space on the 85th floor for radio broadcasts from 1931 on.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014294.E2.80.93295_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014294.E2.80.93295-166">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-be196708_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-be196708-167">[163]</a></sup> On the whole, however, the building was in debt from the outset, losing $1 million per year by 1935. Real estate developer <a href="/wiki/Seymour_Durst" title="Seymour Durst">Seymour Durst</a> recalled that the building was so underused in 1936 that there was no elevator service above the 45th floor, as the building above the 41st floor was empty except for the NBC offices and the Raskob/Du Pont offices on the 81st floor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014295_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014295-168">[164]</a></sup></p>
<p>As originally planned, the Empire State Building's <a href="#Above_the_102nd_floor">spire</a> was supposed to be used as an <a href="/wiki/Airship" title="Airship">airship</a> docking station. Raskob and Smith proposed dirigible ticketing offices and passenger waiting rooms on the 86th floor, while the airships themselves would be tied to the spire at the equivalent of the building's 106th
