floor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200982_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200982-169">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackson20101344_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackson20101344-170">[166]</a></sup> An elevator between the 86th and 101st floors<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101st-floor_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-101st-floor-172">[e]</a></sup> would carry passengers after they checked in on the 86th floor,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hearst_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-hearst-173">[168]</a></sup> with steep ladders leading to the airship's entrance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200982_169-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200982-169">[165]</a></sup> The idea was impractical and dangerous, due to the powerful updrafts caused by the building itself,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldman198044_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldman198044-174">[169]</a></sup> the wind currents across Manhattan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200982_169-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200982-169">[165]</a></sup> and the spires of nearby skyscrapers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smithsonian2000_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-smithsonian2000-175">[170]</a></sup> Furthermore, even if the airship were to successfully navigate all these obstacles, its crew would have to jettison some <a href="/wiki/Ballast" title="Ballast">ballast</a> by releasing water onto the streets below in order to maintain stability, and then tie the craft's nose to the spire with no mooring lines tying the other end of the craft to the ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-Perch-2011_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-Perch-2011-15">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200982_169-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200982-169">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smithsonian2000_175-1"><a href="#cite_note-smithsonian2000-175">[170]</a></sup> On September 15, 1931, in the first and only instance of an airship using the building's mast, a small commercial <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">United States Navy</a> airship circled 25 times in 45 miles per hour (72 km/h) winds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[171]</a></sup> The airship then attempted to dock at the mast, but its ballast spilled and the craft itself was roiled by capricious <a href="/wiki/Eddy_(fluid_dynamics)" title="Eddy (fluid dynamics)">eddies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Associated_Press_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-Associated_Press-177">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[173]</a></sup> The near-disaster scuttled plans to make the building's spire into an airship terminal, although one blimp did manage to make a single newspaper delivery afterward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200982_169-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200982-169">[165]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1932, the Fifth Avenue Association gave the tower its 1931 "gold medal" for architectural excellence, signifying that the Empire State had been the best-designed building on Fifth Avenue to open in 1931.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[174]</a></sup> A year later, on March 2, 1933, the movie <i><a href="/wiki/King_Kong_(1933_film)" title="King Kong (1933 film)">King Kong</a></i> was released. The movie, which depicted a large <a href="/wiki/Stop_motion" title="Stop motion">stop motion</a> ape named <a href="/wiki/King_Kong" title="King Kong">Kong</a> climbing the Empire State Building, made the still-new building into a cinematic icon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac201425_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac201425-180">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200972_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200972-181">[176]</a></sup></p>
<p>To save the building from bankruptcy, the management company of the building opened an observatory on the 86th floor and allowed public access to a large terrace on the eighty-first floor. A <a href="/wiki/Wedding_chapel" title="Wedding chapel">wedding chapel</a> was also added to the building's 80th floor. This made the tower one of the city's major tourist attractions, and during the first years of operation, revenues from tourism equaled the total amount of rent paid by all tenants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199846.26ndash.3B48_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199846.26ndash.3B48-182">[177]</a></sup> The tower received its 5 millionth tourist in 1944.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[178]</a></sup></p>
<p>On July 28, 1945, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/B-25_Mitchell" title="B-25 Mitchell">B-25 Mitchell</a> bomber <a href="/wiki/1945_Empire_State_Building_B-25_crash" title="1945 Empire State Building B-25 crash">crashed</a> into the north side of the Empire State Building, between the 79th and 80th floors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackson2010413.E2.80.93414_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackson2010413.E2.80.93414-184">[179]</a></sup> One engine shot through the side opposite the impact and flew as far as the next block, while the other engine and part of the landing gear plummeted down an elevator shaft. Fourteen people were killed in the incident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBermanMuseum_of_New_York_City200386_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBermanMuseum_of_New_York_City200386-185">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Observer-ESB-Challenged-1976_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-Observer-ESB-Challenged-1976-91">[87]</a></sup> However, the building was not severely damaged or structurally compromised, and it reopened two days later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBermanMuseum_of_New_York_City200386_185-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBermanMuseum_of_New_York_City200386-185">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bde19450730_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-bde19450730-186">[181]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Profitability">Profitability</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:408px;max-width:408px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:View_of_the_Empire_State_Building_from_Macy%27s.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="1944" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/View_of_the_Empire_State_Building_from_Macy%27s.jpg/200px-View_of_the_Empire_State_Building_from_Macy%27s.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/View_of_the_Empire_State_Building_from_Macy%27s.jpg/300px-View_of_the_Empire_State_Building_from_Macy%27s.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/View_of_the_Empire_State_Building_from_Macy%27s.jpg/400px-View_of_the_Empire_State_Building_from_Macy%27s.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">View from <a href="/wiki/Macy%27s" title="Macy's">Macy's</a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Empire_State_Building_background.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="768" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Empire_State_Building_background.jpg/200px-Empire_State_Building_background.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Empire_State_Building_background.jpg/300px-Empire_State_Building_background.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Empire_State_Building_background.jpg/400px-Empire_State_Building_background.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Night view from the same street-corner</div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Empire State Building only became profitable in the 1950s, when it was finally able to <a href="/wiki/Break-even_(economics)" title="Break-even (economics)">break even</a> for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young_Young_2007_p._144_145-4"><a href="#cite_note-Young_Young_2007_p._144-145">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014332_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014332-187">[182]</a></sup> Despite the lack of nearby transportation centers, the Empire State Building started to attract renters due to its reputation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackson20101188_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackson20101188-188">[183]</a></sup> A 222-foot (68 m) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_antenna" title="Radio antenna">radio antenna</a> was erected on top of the tower starting in 1950,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bcd19500728_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-bcd19500728-189">[184]</a></sup> allowing the area's television stations to broadcast from the building.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyr19530401_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyr19530401-190">[185]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, Raskob felt that the tower had been unprofitable for too long, and he put the tower on sale in 1951<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[186]</a></sup> with a minimum asking price of $50 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JamestownJournal-Appraisers-Helped-Sell-1951_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-JamestownJournal-Appraisers-Helped-Sell-1951-192">[187]</a></sup> The property was purchased by business partners <a href="/wiki/Roger_L._Stevens" title="Roger L. Stevens">Roger L. Stevens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Crown" title="Henry Crown">Henry Crown</a>, Alfred R. Glancy and <a href="/wiki/Ben_Tobin" title="Ben Tobin">Ben Tobin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bagli_2013_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bagli_2013-193">[188]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTTobinObit_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTTobinObit-194">[189]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SuntSentObit_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-SuntSentObit-195">[190]</a></sup> The sale was brokered by the Charles F. Noyes Company, a prominent real estate firm in upper Manhattan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JamestownJournal-Appraisers-Helped-Sell-1951_192-1"><a href="#cite_note-JamestownJournal-Appraisers-Helped-Sell-1951-192">[187]</a></sup> for $51 million, the highest price paid for a single structure at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014366_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014366-196">[191]</a></sup> By this time, the Empire State had been fully leased "for several years", with a "waiting list" to lease space in the building.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[192]</a></sup> That year, six news companies started a combined $600,000 annually for use of the <a href="#Broadcast_stations">tower's antenna</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JamestownJournal-Appraisers-Helped-Sell-1951_192-2"><a href="#cite_note-JamestownJournal-Appraisers-Helped-Sell-1951-192">[187]</a></sup> The antenna was completed by 1953.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyr19530401_190-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyr19530401-190">[185]</a></sup> Crown bought out his partners' ownership stakes in 1954, becoming the sole owner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[193]</a></sup> The next year, the <a href="/wiki/American_Society_of_Civil_Engineers" title="American Society of Civil Engineers">American Society of Civil Engineers</a> named the building one of the "Seven Modern Civil Engineering Wonders".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[194]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ASCE_Metropolitan_Section_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-ASCE_Metropolitan_Section-200">[195]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1961, the building was bought by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harry_B._Helmsley" title="Harry B. Helmsley">Harry B. Helmsley</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lawrence_A._Wien" title="Lawrence A. Wien">Lawrence A. Wien</a>, and Wien's son-in-law <a href="/wiki/Peter_L._Malkin" title="Peter L. Malkin">Peter L. Malkin</a>, for $65 million,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19610823_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19610823-201">[196]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bagli_2013_193-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bagli_2013-193">[188]</a></sup> which became the new highest price for a single structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19610823_201-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19610823-201">[196]</a></sup> Over 3,000 people paid $10,000 for one share each in a company called Empire State Building Associates, that subletted the building to another company headed by Helmsley and Wein, raising $33 million of the funds used to pay for the change of ownership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19610823_201-2"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19610823-201">[196]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bagli_2013_193-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bagli_2013-193">[188]</a></sup> The land itself was sold to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prudential_Insurance" title="Prudential Insurance">Prudential Insurance</a> for $29 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[197]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bagli_2013_193-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bagli_2013-193">[188]</a></sup> The new owners quickly started a program of minor improvement projects, including the first-ever full-building facade refurbishment and window-washing in 1962;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014351_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014351-203">[198]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[199]</a></sup> the installation of new flood lights on the 72nd floor in 1964;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014353_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014353-205">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19640121_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19640121-206">[201]</a></sup> and replacement of the manually operated elevators with automatic units in 1966.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014352.E2.80.93353_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014352.E2.80.93353-207">[202]</a></sup> The little-used western end of the second floor was used as a storage space until 1964, at which point it received escalators to the first floor as part of its conversion into a highly-sought retail area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014352_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014352-208">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[204]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id='Loss_of_"tallest_building"_title'></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Loss_of_.22tallest_building.22_title">Loss of "tallest building" title</span></h3>
<p>In 1961, the same year that Helmsley, Wien, and Malkin had purchased the Empire State Building, the <a href="/wiki/Port_Authority_of_New_York_and_New_Jersey" title="Port Authority of New York and New Jersey">Port Authority of New York and New Jersey</a> formally backed plans for a new <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Center_(1973%E2%80%932001)" title="World Trade Center (1973–2001)">World Trade Center</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lower_Manhattan" title="Lower Manhattan">Lower Manhattan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[205]</a></sup> The plan originally included 66-story twin towers with column-free open spaces. The Empire State's owners and real estate speculators were worried that the twin towers' 7,600,000 square feet (710,000 m<sup>2</sup>) of office space would create an excess of rentable space in Manhattan as well as take away the Empire State Building's profits from lessees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014353.E2.80.93354_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014353.E2.80.93354-211">[206]</a></sup> A revision in the World Trade Center's plan brought the twin towers to 1,370 feet (420 m) each or 110 stories, taller than the Empire State.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014354_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014354-212">[207]</a></sup> Opponents of the new project included prominent real-estate developer <a href="/wiki/Robert_Tishman" title="Robert Tishman">Robert Tishman</a>, as well as Wien's Committee for a Reasonable World Trade Center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014354_212-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014354-212">[207]</a></sup> In response to Wein's opposition, Port Authority executive director <a href="/wiki/Austin_J._Tobin" title="Austin J. Tobin">Austin J. Tobin</a> said that Wein was only opposing the project because it would overshadow his Empire State Building as the world's tallest building.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[208]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_Trade_Center,_New_York_City_-_aerial_view_(March_2001).jpg"><img alt="The World Trade Center's Twin Towers as seen from the air" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1924" data-file-width="1361" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/World_Trade_Center%2C_New_York_City_-_aerial_view_%28March_2001%29.jpg/170px-World_Trade_Center%2C_New_York_City_-_aerial_view_%28March_2001%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/World_Trade_Center%2C_New_York_City_-_aerial_view_%28March_2001%29.jpg/255px-World_Trade_Center%2C_New_York_City_-_aerial_view_%28March_2001%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/World_Trade_Center%2C_New_York_City_-_aerial_view_%28March_2001%29.jpg/340px-World_Trade_Center%2C_New_York_City_-_aerial_view_%28March_2001%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_Trade_Center,_New_York_City_-_aerial_view_(March_2001).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Center_(1973%E2%80%932001)" title="World Trade Center (1973–2001)">World Trade Center</a>'s Twin Towers surpassed the Empire State Building in height by 1970.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19701020_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19701020-214">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19701224_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19701224-215">[210]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The World Trade Center's twin towers started <a href="/wiki/Construction_of_the_World_Trade_Center" title="Construction of the World Trade Center">construction</a> in 1966.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[211]</a></sup> The next year, the <a href="/wiki/Ostankino_Tower" title="Ostankino Tower">Ostankino Tower</a> succeeded the Empire State Building as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_freestanding_structures" title="List of tallest freestanding structures">tallest freestanding structure in the world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CBS_News_2012_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-CBS_News_2012-217">[212]</a></sup> The Empire State also lost the title of world's tallest building in 1970,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBermanMuseum_of_New_York_City2003105.E2.80.93106_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBermanMuseum_of_New_York_City2003105.E2.80.93106-218">[213]</a></sup> as the taller North Tower surpassed the Empire State's height on October 19 of that year,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19701020_214-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19701020-214">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19701224_215-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19701224-215">[210]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Topping_out" title="Topping out">topped out</a> on December 23, 1970.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19701224_215-2"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19701224-215">[210]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[214]</a></sup> The Twin Towers opened their 110th-floor observatory in December 1975, which meant that the Empire State Building did not even have the tallest observatory in the city anymore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Observer-ESB-Challenged-1976_91-2"><a href="#cite_note-Observer-ESB-Challenged-1976-91">[87]</a></sup> The Empire State Building also lost income during this time, as many of the broadcast stations moved to the World Trade Center in 1971, although the Port Authority continued to pay the broadcasting leases for the Empire State until 1984.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014356.E2.80.93357_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014356.E2.80.93357-220">[215]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 1980, there were nearly two million annual visitors,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackson20101326_164-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackson20101326-164">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[216]</a></sup> although official estimates the previous year had placed the figure closer to between 1.5 million and 1.75 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oth19791128_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-oth19791128-222">[217]</a></sup> The building received its own ZIP code in May 1980 as part of the addition of 63 new postal codes in Manhattan. At the time, the tower's tenants received a collective 35,000 pieces of mail every day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19800501_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19800501-23">[21]</a></sup> The Empire State Building celebrated its 50th anniversary on May 1, 1981, with a much-publicized laser light show that failed to impress many viewers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[218]</a></sup> as well as an "Empire State Building Week" that ran through May 8.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[219]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELandmarks_Preservation_Commission198116_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELandmarks_Preservation_Commission198116-225">[220]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Landmarks_Preservation_Commission" title="New York City Landmarks Preservation Commission">New York City Landmarks Preservation Commission</a> voted to make the lobby a city landmark on May 19, 1981, citing the historic nature of the first and second floors, as well as "the fixtures and interior components" of the upper floors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELandmarks_Preservation_Commission1981i_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELandmarks_Preservation_Commission1981i-226">[221]</a></sup> The building became a National Historic Landmark in 1986<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nhlsum_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-nhlsum-12">[10]</a></sup> for very similar reasons to the New York City Landmarks report.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nhlnom_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-nhlnom-227">[222]</a></sup> That year, the <a href="/wiki/Plaza_Hotel" title="Plaza Hotel">Plaza Hotel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art" title="Metropolitan Museum of Art">Metropolitan Museum of Art</a> further uptown were also designated as National Historic Landmarks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[223]</a></sup> The Empire State Building was added to the <a href="/wiki/National_Register_of_Historic_Places" title="National Register of Historic Places">National Register of Historic Places</a> the next year due to its architectural significance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[224]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the early and mid-1990s, the alarm systems, elevators, windows, and air conditioning were replaced; the 86th floor observation deck was made accessible to disabled visitors; and the facade was again refurbished<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19960721_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19960721-230">[225]</a></sup> at a cost of $55 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19940708_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19940708-231">[226]</a></sup> Prudential sold the land under the building in 1991 for $42 million to a buyer representing hotelier <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Hideki_Yokoi&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Hideki Yokoi (page does not exist)">Hideki Yokoi</a>, who was at the time imprisoned in connection with a deadly fire at the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Hotel_New_Japan&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Hotel New Japan (page does not exist)">Hotel New Japan</a> hotel in <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[227]</a></sup> The land was bought jointly by <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a> and Hideki Yokoi in 1994.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[228]</a></sup> Having secured a half-ownership of the land, Trump devised plans to take ownership of the building itself so he could renovate it, even though Helmsley and Malkin had already started their refurbishment project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19940708_231-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19940708-231">[226]</a></sup> He sued Empire State Building Associates in February 1995, claiming that the latter had caused the building to become a "high-rise slum"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bagli_2013_193-4"><a href="#cite_note-Bagli_2013-193">[188]</a></sup> and a "second-rate, rodent-infested" office tower.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19950217_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19950217-234">[229]</a></sup> Trump had aimed to get Empire State Building Associates evicted by breaking their lease,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19950217_234-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19950217-234">[229]</a></sup> but that action was denied,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[230]</a></sup> and Helmsley's companies sued Trump in May of that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[231]</a></sup> This sparked a round of lawsuits and countersuits that lasted several years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bagli_2013_193-5"><a href="#cite_note-Bagli_2013-193">[188]</a></sup> which partially arose from Trump's desire to obtain the building's master lease by taking it from Empire State Building Associates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19960721_230-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19960721-230">[225]</a></sup> Upon Harry Helmsley's death in 1997, the Malkins sued Helmsley's widow <a href="/wiki/Leona_Helmsley" title="Leona Helmsley">Leona Helmsley</a> for ownership of the building.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[232]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="After_9/11"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="After_9.2F11">After 9/11</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_night_view_from_the_observatory.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1760" data-file-width="2640" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/A_night_view_from_the_observatory.jpg/220px-A_night_view_from_the_observatory.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/A_night_view_from_the_observatory.jpg/330px-A_night_view_from_the_observatory.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/A_night_view_from_the_observatory.jpg/440px-A_night_view_from_the_observatory.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_night_view_from_the_observatory.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A night view from the observatory, looking south</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The World Trade Center was destroyed in the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a> in 2001. The Empire State Building again became the <a href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_buildings_in_New_York_City" title="List of tallest buildings in New York City">tallest building in New York City</a>, but was now only the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_buildings_in_the_world" title="List of tallest buildings in the world">second-tallest building in the Americas</a>, surpassed by the <a href="/wiki/Willis_Tower" title="Willis Tower">Willis Tower</a> in Chicago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunlap_2012_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dunlap_2012-238">[233]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CBS_News_2012_217-1"><a href="#cite_note-CBS_News_2012-217">[212]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nydn20120430_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-nydn20120430-239">[234]</a></sup> Because of the September 11 attacks, nearly all of the city's commercial television and FM radio broadcast stations started transmitting again from the top of the Empire State Building.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuerrero_et_al.200237_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuerrero_et_al.200237-240">[235]</a></sup> In the aftermath of the attacks, security measures at the Empire State Building were also increased due to persistent terror threats against New York City landmarks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[236]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2002, Trump and Yokoi sold their claim to the land to the Empire State Building Associates, now headed by Malkin in a $57.5 million sale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt20020319_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt20020319-242">[237]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bagli_2013_193-6"><a href="#cite_note-Bagli_2013-193">[188]</a></sup> This action merged the building's title and lease for the first time in a half-century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt20020319_242-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyt20020319-242">[237]</a></sup> Despite the lingering threat posed by the 9/11 attacks, the Empire State Building remained popular, with 3.5 million visitors to the observatories in 2004 compared to about 2.8 million in 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[238]</a></sup></p>
<p>Leona Helmsley's remaining share in the building was bought by Peter Malkin's company in 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[239]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bagli_2013_193-7"><a href="#cite_note-Bagli_2013-193">[188]</a></sup> In 2008 the building was temporary “stolen” by the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Daily_News" title="New York Daily News">New York Daily News</a> to show how easy it is to transfer the deed on a property, since city clerks were not required to validate the submitted information, as well as to help demonstrate how fraudulent deeds could be used to obtain large mortgages and then have individuals disappear with the money. The paperwork submitted to the city included the names of <a href="/wiki/Fay_Wray" title="Fay Wray">Fay Wray</a>, the famous star of <i>King Kong</i>, and <a href="/wiki/Willie_Sutton" title="Willie Sutton">Willie Sutton</a>, a notorious New York bank robber. The newspaper then transferred the deed back over to the legitimate owners, who at that time were Empire State Land Associates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[240]</a></sup></p>
<p>The building's public areas received a $550 million renovation in 2009 (see <a href="#Major_renovations">§ Major renovations</a>). The building received new air conditioning, waterproofing, a renovated observation deck and lobby, and a relocated 80th-floor gift shop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cortese_2008_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cortese_2008-246">[241]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esbnyc1_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-esbnyc1-247">[242]</a></sup> This included $120 million of <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">energy efficient</a> upgrades,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esbnyc1_247-1"><a href="#cite_note-esbnyc1-247">[242]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Navarro_2009_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-Navarro_2009-248">[243]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014368_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014368-249">[244]</a></sup> which allowed the building to receive a gold <a href="/wiki/Leadership_in_Energy_and_Environmental_Design" title="Leadership in Energy and Environmental Design">Leadership in Energy and Environmental Design (LEED)</a> rating in September 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inhabitat1_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-inhabitat1-250">[245]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-251">[246]</a></sup> The new One World Trade Center surpassed the Empire State Building as the tallest LEED-certified building when it opened in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014368_249-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014368-249">[244]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of 2014<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Empire_State_Building&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> the building is owned by the Empire State Realty Trust with Anthony Malkin as Chairman, CEO, and President.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[247]</a></sup> Details on the trust's profits are scarce, but it gained significantly more revenue from tourism than from leasing the office space in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt20111225_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt20111225-253">[248]</a></sup> In August 2016, the <a href="/wiki/Qatar_Investment_Authority" title="Qatar Investment Authority">Qatar Investment Authority</a> (QIA) gained a 10% share in the Empire State Building through a $622 million investment to the Empire State Realty Trust. The trust’s president John Kessler called it an “endorsement of the company’s irreplaceable assets”.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-254">[249]</a></sup> The investment has been described as “an unusual move for a sovereign wealth fund”, as they typically buy direct stakes in buildings rather than real estate companies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[250]</a></sup> Other foreign entities that have a stake in the Empire State Building include investors from <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_254-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-254">[249]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Empirestatebuildingfrombrooklynnewyork.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="426" height="331" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Empirestatebuildingfrombrooklynnewyork.jpg/220px-Empirestatebuildingfrombrooklynnewyork.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Empirestatebuildingfrombrooklynnewyork.jpg/330px-Empirestatebuildingfrombrooklynnewyork.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9a/Empirestatebuildingfrombrooklynnewyork.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Empirestatebuildingfrombrooklynnewyork.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Empire State Building as seen across the <a href="/wiki/East_River" title="East River">East River</a>, from <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn" title="Brooklyn">Brooklyn</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Empire State Building rises to 1,250 ft (381 m) at the 102nd floor, and including the 203 ft (61.9 m) pinnacle, its full height reaches 1,453 feet <span class="frac nowrap">8<span class="visualhide"> </span><sup>9</sup>⁄<sub>16</sub></span> inches (443.092 m).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199814_80-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199814-80">[76]</a></sup> At that distance, the tower is visible from the ocean up to 50 miles (80 km) away, from upstate up to 70 miles (110 km) away, and from an airplane up to 100 miles (160 km) away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPopular_MechanicsDecember_1930920_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPopular_MechanicsDecember_1930920-256">[251]</a></sup></p>
<p>The building has 85 stories of commercial and office space representing 2,158,000 sq ft (200,500 m<sup>2</sup>) of rentable space. It has an indoor and outdoor observation deck on the 86th floor, the highest floor within the actual tower.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199814_80-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199814-80">[76]</a></sup> The remaining 16 stories represent the <a href="/wiki/Art_Deco" title="Art Deco">Art Deco</a> spire, which is capped by a 102nd-floor observatory. The spire is hollow, and there are no floors between levels 86 and 102.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199814_80-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199814-80">[76]</a></sup> Atop the tower is the 203 ft (61.9 m) pinnacle, much of which is covered by broadcast antennas, with a <a href="/wiki/Lightning_rod" title="Lightning rod">lightning rod</a> at the very top.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackson2010413.E2.80.93414_184-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackson2010413.E2.80.93414-184">[179]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Empire State Building cost $40,948,900 to build, including demolition of the Waldorf-Astoria (equivalent to $644,878,000 in 2016). This was lower than the $60 million budgeted for construction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cost_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-cost-5">[4]</a></sup> Official fact sheets cite building statistics thusly: an ascent of 1,860 steps from the first to the 102nd floor; a weight of 365,000 short tons (331,122 t); a volume of 37,000,000 cubic feet (1,000,000 m<sup>3</sup>); and an exterior with 200,000 cubic feet (5,700 m<sup>3</sup>) of limestone and granite, along with ten million bricks and 370 short tons (330 long tons) of steel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[252]</a></sup> Other oft-cited statistics include 1,172 miles (1,886 km) of elevator cable and 2,000,000 feet (609,600 m) of electrical wires,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[253]</a></sup> as well as capacity for 20,000 tenants and 15,000 visitors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPopular_MechanicsDecember_1930920_256-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPopular_MechanicsDecember_1930920-256">[251]</a></sup></p>
<p>The building has been named as one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seven_Wonders_of_the_Modern_World" title="Seven Wonders of the Modern World">Seven Wonders of the Modern World</a> by the <a href="/wiki/American_Society_of_Civil_Engineers" title="American Society of Civil Engineers">American Society of Civil Engineers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[254]</a></sup> The building and its street floor interior are designated landmarks of the <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Landmarks_Preservation_Commission" title="New York City Landmarks Preservation Commission">New York City Landmarks Preservation Commission</a>, and confirmed by the <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Board_of_Estimate" title="New York City Board of Estimate">New York City Board of Estimate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AIA_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-AIA-260">[255]</a></sup> It was designated as a <a href="/wiki/National_Historic_Landmark" title="National Historic Landmark">National Historic Landmark</a> in 1986.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nhlsum_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-nhlsum-12">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nhlnom_227-1"><a href="#cite_note-nhlnom-227">[222]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nrhpphotos_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-nrhpphotos-261">[256]</a></sup> In 2007, it was ranked number one on the AIA's List of <a href="/wiki/America%27s_Favorite_Architecture" title="America's Favorite Architecture">America's Favorite Architecture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[257]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exterior">Exterior</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Looking_Up_at_Empire_State_Building.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Looking_Up_at_Empire_State_Building.JPG/220px-Looking_Up_at_Empire_State_Building.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Looking_Up_at_Empire_State_Building.JPG/330px-Looking_Up_at_Empire_State_Building.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Looking_Up_at_Empire_State_Building.JPG/440px-Looking_Up_at_Empire_State_Building.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Looking_Up_at_Empire_State_Building.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A series of <a href="/wiki/Setback_(architecture)" title="Setback (architecture)">setbacks</a> causes the building to taper with height.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Empire State Building's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Art_deco" title="Art deco">art deco</a> design is typical of pre–World War II architecture in New York. The modernistic <a href="/wiki/Stainless_steel" title="Stainless steel">stainless steel</a> canopies of the entrances on 33rd and 34th Streets lead to two-story-high corridors around the elevator core, crossed by stainless steel and glass-enclosed bridges at the second-floor level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AIA_260-1"><a href="#cite_note-AIA-260">[255]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Rivet" title="Rivet">riveted</a> steel frame of the building was originally designed to handle all of the building's gravity stresses and wind loads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaranath2016459_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaranath2016459-263">[258]</a></sup> The exterior of the building is clad in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indiana_limestone" title="Indiana limestone">Indiana limestone</a> panels, which came from the Empire Mill in <a href="/wiki/Sanders,_Indiana" title="Sanders, Indiana">Sanders, Indiana</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PetersonNYT1981_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-PetersonNYT1981-264">[259]</a></sup> and give the building its signature blonde color.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAl-Kodmany201772_71-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAl-Kodmany201772-71">[67]</a></sup> The limestone facade, which is lined with vertically aligned steel <a href="/wiki/Mullion" title="Mullion">mullions</a> parallel to each window,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200981_70-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200981-70">[66]</a></sup> also makes the steel frame even stiffer against the winds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaranath2016459_263-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaranath2016459-263">[258]</a></sup> The large amount of material used in the building's construction made it very stiff compared to other skyscrapers, with a structural stiffness of 42 pounds per square foot (2.0 kPa) versus the <a href="/wiki/Willis_Tower" title="Willis Tower">Willis Tower</a>'s 33 pounds per square foot (1.6 kPa) and the <a href="/wiki/John_Hancock_Center" title="John Hancock Center">John Hancock Center</a>'s 26 pounds per square foot (1.2 kPa).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaranath2016527_265-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaranath2016527-265">[260]</a></sup> A December 1930 feature in <i><a href="/wiki/Popular_Mechanics" title="Popular Mechanics">Popular Mechanics</a></i> estimated that a building with the Empire State's dimensions would still stand even if hit with an impact of 50 short tons (45 long tons).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPopular_MechanicsDecember_1930920_256-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPopular_MechanicsDecember_1930920-256">[251]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Empire State Building contains one major <a href="/wiki/Setback_(architecture)" title="Setback (architecture)">setback</a> and several smaller ones that cause the floors to shrink as the height increases, which make the higher 81 floors much smaller than the lower five floors but allow the top floors to be both illuminated by sunlight and positioned away from the noisy streets below.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014157_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014157-78">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaydenMunicipal_Art_Society20008.E2.80.939_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaydenMunicipal_Art_Society20008.E2.80.939-266">[261]</a></sup> This design was mandated as per the <a href="/wiki/1916_Zoning_Resolution" title="1916 Zoning Resolution">1916 Zoning Resolution</a>, which was intended to allow sunlight to reach the streets as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zoning_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-zoning-270">[f]</a></sup> Normally, a building of the Empire State's dimensions would be permitted to build up to 12 stories on the Fifth Avenue side, and up to 17 stories on the 33rd/34th Streets side, before it would have to utilize setbacks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-Novel-Design-1930_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-Novel-Design-1930-271">[265]</a></sup> However, the setbacks were arranged such that the largest setback was on the sixth floor, above the five-floor "base",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-Novel-Design-1930_271-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-Novel-Design-1930-271">[265]</a></sup> so the rest of the building above the sixth floor would have a facade of uniform shape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPopular_MechanicsDecember_1930920_256-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPopular_MechanicsDecember_1930920-256">[251]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWillis199596_272-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillis199596-272">[266]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200981_70-9"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200981-70">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interior">Interior</span></h3>
<p>The Empire State Building was the first building to have more than 100 floors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199856_152-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199856-152">[148]</a></sup> It has 6,500 windows; 73 elevators;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Navarro_2009_248-1"><a href="#cite_note-Navarro_2009-248">[243]</a></sup> a total floor area of 2,768,591 sq ft (257,211 m<sup>2</sup>); and a base covering 2 acres (8,094 m<sup>2</sup>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014183_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014183-273">[267]</a></sup> Its original 64 elevators, built by the <a href="/wiki/Otis_Elevator_Company" title="Otis Elevator Company">Otis Elevator Company</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014183_273-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014183-273">[267]</a></sup> are located in a central core and are of varying heights, with the longest of these elevators reaching from the lobby to the 80th floor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-Novel-Design-1930_271-2"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-Novel-Design-1930-271">[265]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bonnier193104_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bonnier193104-274">[268]</a></sup> As originally built, there were four "express" elevators that connected the lobby, 80th floor, and several landings in between; the other 60 "local" elevators connected the landings with the floors above these intermediate landings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWillis199596_272-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillis199596-272">[266]</a></sup> Of the 64 total elevators, 58 were for passenger use (comprising the four express elevators and 54 local elevators), and eight were for freight deliveries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200981_70-10"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200981-70">[66]</a></sup> The elevators were designed to move at 1,200 feet per minute (366 m/min). At the time of the skyscraper's construction, their practical speed was limited to 700 feet per minute (213 m/min) as per city law, but this limit was removed shortly after the building opened.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014183_273-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014183-273">[267]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200981_70-11"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200981-70">[66]</a></sup> Additional elevators connect the 80th floor to the six floors above it, as the six extra floors were built after the original 80 stories were approved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199814_80-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199814-80">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPopular_MechanicsDecember_1930921_275-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPopular_MechanicsDecember_1930921-275">[269]</a></sup> The Empire State Building has 73 elevators in all, including service elevators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaranath2016459_263-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaranath2016459-263">[258]</a></sup> Utilities are grouped in a central shaft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-Novel-Design-1930_271-3"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-Novel-Design-1930-271">[265]</a></sup></p>
<p>On each floor between levels 6 and 86, the central shaft is surrounded by a main corridor on all four sides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200981_70-12"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200981-70">[66]</a></sup> As per the final specifications of the building, the corridor is surrounded in turn by office space 28 feet (8.5 m) deep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWillis199595_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillis199595-92">[88]</a></sup> Each of the floors has 210 structural columns that pass through it, which provide a structural stability but impede the creation of any significant open space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200981_70-13"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200981-70">[66]</a></sup> However, the relative dearth of stone in the building allows for more space overall, with a 1:200 stone-to-building ratio in the Empire State compared to a 1:50 ratio in similar buildings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPopular_MechanicsDecember_1930922_121-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPopular_MechanicsDecember_1930922-121">[117]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lobby">Lobby</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Empire_State_Building_in_Manhattan,_New_York,_USA_(9892744176).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1536" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Empire_State_Building_in_Manhattan%2C_New_York%2C_USA_%289892744176%29.jpg/170px-Empire_State_Building_in_Manhattan%2C_New_York%2C_USA_%289892744176%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Empire_State_Building_in_Manhattan%2C_New_York%2C_USA_%289892744176%29.jpg/255px-Empire_State_Building_in_Manhattan%2C_New_York%2C_USA_%289892744176%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Empire_State_Building_in_Manhattan%2C_New_York%2C_USA_%289892744176%29.jpg/340px-Empire_State_Building_in_Manhattan%2C_New_York%2C_USA_%289892744176%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Empire_State_Building_in_Manhattan,_New_York,_USA_(9892744176).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Aluminum relief of the building</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The main lobby is accessed from Fifth Avenue, on the building's east side, and contains an entrance with one set of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Double_door&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Double door (page does not exist)">double doors</a> between a pair of <a href="/wiki/Revolving_door" title="Revolving door">revolving doors</a>. At the top of each doorway is a bronze motif of three of the "crafts or industries" used in the building's construction—Electricity, Masonry, and Heating from north to south.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELandmarks_Preservation_Commission198113_276-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELandmarks_Preservation_Commission198113-276">[270]</a></sup> The lobby contains two tiers of marble: a lighter marble on the top, above the storefronts, and a darker marble on the bottom, flush with the storefronts. There is a pattern of zigzagging <a href="/wiki/Terrazzo" title="Terrazzo">terrazzo</a> tiles on the lobby floor, which leads from the entrance on the east to the aluminum relief on the west.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELandmarks_Preservation_Commission198115_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELandmarks_Preservation_Commission198115-277">[271]</a></sup> The chapel-like three-story-high lobby, which runs parallel to 33rd and 34th Streets, contains storefronts on both its northern and southern sides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELandmarks_Preservation_Commission198112.E2.80.9313_278-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELandmarks_Preservation_Commission198112.E2.80.9313-278">[272]</a></sup> These storefronts are framed by tubes of dark, "modernistically rounded marble", and above the storefronts is a vertical band of grooves set into the marble.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELandmarks_Preservation_Commission198115_277-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELandmarks_Preservation_Commission198115-277">[271]</a></sup> Immediately inside the lobby is an airport-style security checkpoint.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFodor.27s2010153_279-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFodor.27s2010153-279">[273]</a></sup></p>
<p>The walls on both the northern and southern sides of the lobby each contain storefronts and escalators to a mezzanine level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELandmarks_Preservation_Commission198115_277-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELandmarks_Preservation_Commission198115-277">[271]</a></sup> At the west end of the lobby is an aluminum relief of the skyscraper as it was originally built (i.e. without the antenna).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lepik2008_280-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lepik2008-280">[274]</a></sup> The relief, which was intended to provide a welcoming effect,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELandmarks_Preservation_Commission19811_281-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELandmarks_Preservation_Commission19811-281">[275]</a></sup> contains an embossing of the building's outline "with the rays of an aluminum sun shining out behind it and mingling with aluminum rays emanating from the spire of the Empire State Building". In the background is a state map of New York, with the building's location marked by a "medallion" in the very southeast portion of the outline. A compass is located in the bottom right, and a plaque to the tower's major developers is on the bottom left.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELandmarks_Preservation_Commission198114_282-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELandmarks_Preservation_Commission198114-282">[276]</a></sup> At this point, the lobby feeds into a one-story tall, similarly-designed rectangular-shaped corridor that surrounds the banks of escalators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELandmarks_Preservation_Commission198112.2C_15_283-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELandmarks_Preservation_Commission198112.2C_15-283">[277]</a></sup> The rectangular shaped corridor actually consists of two long hallways on the northern and southern sides of the rectangle,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELandmarks_Preservation_Commission198112_284-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELandmarks_Preservation_Commission198112-284">[278]</a></sup> as well as a shorter hallway on the eastern side and another long hallway on the western side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELandmarks_Preservation_Commission198112.2C_15_283-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELandmarks_Preservation_Commission198112.2C_15-283">[277]</a></sup> At both ends of the northern and southern corridors, there is one bank of four low-rise elevators in between the corridors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELandmarks_Preservation_Commission198116_225-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELandmarks_Preservation_Commission198116-225">[220]</a></sup> The western side of the rectangular elevator-bank corridor extends north to the 34th Street entrance and south to the 33rd Street entrance. It borders three large storefronts and leads to escalators that go both to the second floor and to the basement. Going from west to east, there are secondary entrances to 34th and 33rd Streets from both the northern and southern corridors, respectively, at approximately the two-thirds point of each corridor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELandmarks_Preservation_Commission198115_277-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELandmarks_Preservation_Commission198115-277">[271]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285">[g]</a></sup></p>
<p>Until the 1960s, the ceilings in the lobby had a shiny art deco mural inspired by both the sky and the <a href="/wiki/Machine_Age" title="Machine Age">Machine Age</a>, until it was covered with ceiling tiles and fluorescent lighting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lepik2008_280-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lepik2008-280">[274]</a></sup> Because the original murals, designed by an artist named Leif Neandross, were damaged, reproductions were installed. Renovations to the lobby alluded to original plans for the building; replacing the clock over the information desk in the Fifth Avenue lobby with an <a href="/wiki/Anemometer" title="Anemometer">anemometer</a>, as well as installing two chandeliers originally intended to be part of the building when it first opened.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt20090923_286-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt20090923-286">[279]</a></sup> The north corridor contained eight illuminated panels, created by Roy Sparkia and Renée Nemorov in 1963 in time for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1964_World%27s_Fair" title="1964 World's Fair">1964 World's Fair</a>, which depicts the building as the <a href="/wiki/Eighth_Wonder_of_the_World" title="Eighth Wonder of the World">Eighth Wonder of the World</a>, alongside the traditional seven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELandmarks_Preservation_Commission198116_225-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELandmarks_Preservation_Commission198116-225">[220]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arbus-bio_287-0"><a href="#cite_note-arbus-bio-287">[280]</a></sup> The building's owners installed a series of paintings by the New York artist <a href="/wiki/Kysa_Johnson" title="Kysa Johnson">Kysa Johnson</a> in the concourse level. Johnson filed a federal lawsuit in January 2014 under the <a href="/wiki/Visual_Artists_Rights_Act" title="Visual Artists Rights Act">Visual Artists Rights Act</a>, alleging the negligent destruction of the paintings and damage to her reputation as an artist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[281]</a></sup> As part of the building's 2010 renovation, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Denise_Amses&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Denise Amses (page does not exist)">Denise Amses</a> commissioned a work consisting of "15,000 stars and 5,000 circles" superimposed on a 13-by-5-foot (4.0 by 1.5 m) <a href="/wiki/Glass_etching" title="Glass etching">etched-glass</a> installation in the lobby.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-289"><a href="#cite_note-289">[282]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_renovations">Major renovations</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Manhattan_at_Dusk_by_slonecker.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="1600" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Manhattan_at_Dusk_by_slonecker.jpg/170px-Manhattan_at_Dusk_by_slonecker.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Manhattan_at_Dusk_by_slonecker.jpg/255px-Manhattan_at_Dusk_by_slonecker.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Manhattan_at_Dusk_by_slonecker.jpg/340px-Manhattan_at_Dusk_by_slonecker.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Manhattan_at_Dusk_by_slonecker.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Seen in 2005</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Capital improvements were made to the Empire State Building during the early to mid-1990s at a cost of $55 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19940708_231-2"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19940708-231">[226]</a></sup> These improvements entailed replacing alarm systems, elevators, windows, and air conditioning; making the observation deck compliant with the <a href="/wiki/Americans_with_Disabilities_Act_of_1990" title="Americans with Disabilities Act of 1990">Americans with Disabilities Act of 1990</a> (ADA); and refurbishing the limestone facade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19960721_230-2"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19960721-230">[225]</a></sup> The observatory renovation was added after disability rights groups and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice" title="United States Department of Justice">United States Department of Justice</a> filed a lawsuit against the building in 1992, in what was believed to be the first lawsuit filed by an organization under the then-new ADA law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-290"><a href="#cite_note-290">[283]</a></sup> A settlement was reached in 1994, in which the Empire State Building Associates agreed to add ADA-compliant elements, such as new elevators, ramps, and automatic doors, during its ongoing renovation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-291"><a href="#cite_note-291">[284]</a></sup></p>
<p>The building's public areas received a $550 million renovation in 2009, with improvements including new air conditioning, waterproofing, and renovating the observation deck, and moving the gift shop to the 80th floor,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cortese_2008_246-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cortese_2008-246">[241]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esbnyc1_247-2"><a href="#cite_note-esbnyc1-247">[242]</a></sup> as well as refurbishing the main lobby.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt20090923_286-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyt20090923-286">[279]</a></sup> Of this, $120 million was spent in an effort to transform the building into a more <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">energy efficient</a> and eco-friendly structure, with the goal of reducing energy emissions by 38% within five years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esbnyc1_247-3"><a href="#cite_note-esbnyc1-247">[242]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Navarro_2009_248-2"><a href="#cite_note-Navarro_2009-248">[243]</a></sup> For example, all of the windows were refurbished onsite into film-coated "superwindows" which block heat but pass light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fa2012_292-0"><a href="#cite_note-fa2012-292">[285]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Navarro_2009_248-3"><a href="#cite_note-Navarro_2009-248">[243]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014368_249-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014368-249">[244]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Air_conditioning" title="Air conditioning">Air conditioning</a> operating costs on hot days were reduced, saving $17 million of the project's capital cost immediately and partially funding some of the other retrofits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fa2012_292-1"><a href="#cite_note-fa2012-292">[285]</a></sup> The Empire State Building won a gold <a href="/wiki/Leadership_in_Energy_and_Environmental_Design" title="Leadership in Energy and Environmental Design">Leadership in Energy and Environmental Design (LEED)</a> rating in September 2011,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_251-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-251">[246]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inhabitat1_250-1"><a href="#cite_note-inhabitat1-250">[245]</a></sup> as well as the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=World_Federation_of_Great_Towers&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="World Federation of Great Towers (page does not exist)">World Federation of Great Towers</a>' Excellence in Environment Award for 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014368_249-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014368-249">[244]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Features">Features</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Above_the_102nd_floor"><span id="Dirigible_.28airship.29_terminal"></span>Above the 102nd floor</span></h4>
<p>The final plan for the building called for the installation of a hollow mast, complete of 158-foot (48 m) steel shaft with elevators and utilities, above the 86th floor. At the top would be a conical roof and the 102nd-floor docking station.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014186_293-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014186-293">[286]</a></sup> The elevators would ascend 167 feet (51 m) from the 86th floor ticket offices to a 33-foot-wide (10 m) 101st-floor<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101st-floor_172-1"><a href="#cite_note-101st-floor-172">[e]</a></sup> waiting room.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hearst_173-1"><a href="#cite_note-hearst-173">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200982_169-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200982-169">[165]</a></sup> From there, stairs would lead to the 102nd floor,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101st-floor_172-2"><a href="#cite_note-101st-floor-172">[e]</a></sup> where passengers would enter the airships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014186_293-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014186-293">[286]</a></sup> The airships would have been moored to the spire at the equivalent of the building's 106th floor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200982_169-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200982-169">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackson20101344_170-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackson20101344-170">[166]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the 102nd floor of the Empire State Building (formerly the 101st floor), there is a door with stairs ascending to the 103rd floor (formerly the 102nd).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101st-floor_172-3"><a href="#cite_note-101st-floor-172">[e]</a></sup> This was built as a disembarkation floor for airships tethered to the building's spire, and has a circular balcony outside.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-Perch-2011_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-Perch-2011-15">[13]</a></sup> It is now an access point to reach the spire for maintenance. The room now contains electrical equipment, but celebrities and dignitaries may also be given permission to take pictures there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CBS_New_York_2011_294-0"><a href="#cite_note-CBS_New_York_2011-294">[287]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-295"><a href="#cite_note-295">[288]</a></sup> Above the 103rd floor, there is a set of stairs and a ladder to reach the spire for maintenance work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CBS_New_York_2011_294-1"><a href="#cite_note-CBS_New_York_2011-294">[287]</a></sup> The mast's 480 windows were all replaced in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[289]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Broadcast_stations">Broadcast stations</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Empirestatebuilding29122005.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3456" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Empirestatebuilding29122005.JPG/220px-Empirestatebuilding29122005.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Empirestatebuilding29122005.JPG/330px-Empirestatebuilding29122005.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Empirestatebuilding29122005.JPG/440px-Empirestatebuilding29122005.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Empirestatebuilding29122005.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Antennae for broadcast stations are located at the top of the Empire State Building.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Broadcasting began at the Empire State Building on December 22, 1931, when <a href="/wiki/NBC" title="NBC">NBC</a> and <a href="/wiki/RCA" title="RCA">RCA</a> began transmitting experimental television broadcasts from a small antenna erected atop the spire, with two separate transmitters for the visual and audio data. They leased the 85th floor and built a laboratory there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-be196708_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-be196708-167">[163]</a></sup> In 1934, RCA was joined by <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Howard_Armstrong" title="Edwin Howard Armstrong">Edwin Howard Armstrong</a> in a cooperative venture to test his FM system from the building's antenna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZarkinZarkin2006_297-0"><a href="#cite_note-ZarkinZarkin2006-297">[290]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lessing_1956_p._20_298-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lessing_1956_p._20-298">[291]</a></sup> This setup, which entailed the installation of the world's first <a href="/wiki/Frequency_modulation" title="Frequency modulation">FM transmitter</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lessing_1956_p._20_298-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lessing_1956_p._20-298">[291]</a></sup> continued only until October of the next year because of disputes between RCA and Armstrong.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-be196708_167-2"><a href="#cite_note-be196708-167">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZarkinZarkin2006_297-1"><a href="#cite_note-ZarkinZarkin2006-297">[290]</a></sup> Specifically, NBC wanted to install more TV equipment in the room where Armstrong's transmitter was located.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lessing_1956_p._20_298-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lessing_1956_p._20-298">[291]</a></sup></p>
<p>Afterward, the 85th floor became the home of RCA's New York television operations, first as experimental station W2XBS channel 1, then as commercial station WNBT, channel 1 (now <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WNBC-TV" title="WNBC-TV">WNBC-TV</a> channel 4) from July 1941 on. NBC's FM station W2XDG, began transmitting from the antenna in 1940.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-be196708_167-3"><a href="#cite_note-be196708-167">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-299"><a href="#cite_note-299">[292]</a></sup> NBC retained exclusive use of the top of the building until 1950, when the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Communications_Commission" title="Federal Communications Commission">FCC</a> ordered the exclusive deal broken, based on consumer complaints that a common location was necessary for the seven extant New York-area television stations to transmit from so that receiving antennas would not have to be constantly adjusted. Construction on a giant tower began on July 27, 1950.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bcd19500728_189-1"><a href="#cite_note-bcd19500728-189">[184]</a></sup> Other television broadcasters then joined RCA at the building, on the 83rd, 82nd, and 81st floors, often along with sister FM stations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-be196708_167-4"><a href="#cite_note-be196708-167">[163]</a></sup> Multiple transmissions of TV and FM began from the new tower in 1951, and was completed by 1953.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAl-Kodmany201772_71-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAl-Kodmany201772-71">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dupre_2013_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-Dupre_2013-79">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyr19530401_190-2"><a href="#cite_note-nyr19530401-190">[185]</a></sup> Six broadcasters paid a combined $600,000 for the use of the antenna in 1951 per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JamestownJournal-Appraisers-Helped-Sell-1951_192-3"><a href="#cite_note-JamestownJournal-Appraisers-Helped-Sell-1951-192">[187]</a></sup> In 1965, a separate set of FM antennas was constructed ringing the 103rd floor observation area to act as a master antenna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-be196708_167-5"><a href="#cite_note-be196708-167">[163]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Empire_State_Building_from_the_Top_of_the_Rock.jpg"><img alt="The Empire State Building as seen from the Top of the Rock" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1014" data-file-width="756" height="295" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Empire_State_Building_from_the_Top_of_the_Rock.jpg/220px-Empire_State_Building_from_the_Top_of_the_Rock.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Empire_State_Building_from_the_Top_of_the_Rock.jpg/330px-Empire_State_Building_from_the_Top_of_the_Rock.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Empire_State_Building_from_the_Top_of_the_Rock.jpg/440px-Empire_State_Building_from_the_Top_of_the_Rock.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Empire_State_Building_from_the_Top_of_the_Rock.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Empire State Building as seen from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Top_of_the_Rock" title="Top of the Rock">Top of the Rock</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The stations' placement in the Empire State Building became a major issue with the construction of the World Trade Center's Twin Towers during the late 1960s and early 1970s. As the towers were taller than the Empire State Building, this would cause waves from the Empire State Building to sometimes bounce off the walls of the taller Twin Towers. As a result, some broadcasters decided that it would be easier to move to the Twin Towers instead, rather than suing the complex's developer, the <a href="/wiki/Port_Authority_of_New_York_and_New_Jersey" title="Port Authority of New York and New Jersey">Port Authority of New York and New Jersey</a>, to stop construction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014356_300-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014356-300">[293]</a></sup> Even though the nine stations who were broadcasting from the Empire State Building were leasing their broadcast space until 1984, most of these stations moved to the World Trade Center as soon as it was completed in 1971. The broadcasters obtained a court order stipulating that the Port Authority had to build a mast and transmission equipment in the <a href="/wiki/One_World_Trade_Center#Original_building" title="One World Trade Center">North Tower</a>, as well as pay the broadcasters' leases in the Empire State Building until 1984.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014356.E2.80.93357_220-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014356.E2.80.93357-220">[215]</a></sup> After the leases expired, there were very few broadcasters remaining in the Empire State Building.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014357_301-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014357-301">[294]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a> in 2001 destroyed the World Trade Center and the broadcast centers atop it, leaving most of the city's stations without a station for ten days until a temporary tower was built in <a href="/wiki/Alpine,_New_Jersey" title="Alpine, New Jersey">Alpine, New Jersey</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuerrero_et_al.200236_302-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuerrero_et_al.200236-302">[295]</a></sup> By October 2001, nearly all of the city's commercial broadcast stations (both television and FM radio) were transmitting from the top of the Empire State Building. The stations' placement in the Empire State Building was considered "problematic" due to interference from other nearby towers, as contrasted with the former Twin Towers, which had very few buildings of comparable height nearby.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuerrero_et_al.200237_240-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuerrero_et_al.200237-240">[235]</a></sup> In 2003, a few FM stations were relocated to the nearby <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cond%C3%A9_Nast_Building" title="Condé Nast Building">Condé Nast Building</a> to reduce the number of broadcast stations using the Empire State Building.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303">[296]</a></sup> Eleven <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Television_broadcasting" title="Television broadcasting">television</a> stations and 22 <a href="/wiki/FM_broadcasting" title="FM broadcasting">FM</a> stations had signed 15-year leases in the building by May 2003. It was expected that a taller broadcast tower in <a href="/wiki/Bayonne,_New_Jersey" title="Bayonne, New Jersey">Bayonne, New Jersey</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Governors_Island" title="Governors Island">Governors Island</a> would be built in the meantime, with the Empire State Building being used as a "backup" since signal transmission from the Empire State Building was still of poor quality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-304"><a href="#cite_note-304">[297]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of 2017<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Empire_State_Building&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the Empire State Building is home to the following stations:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-305"><a href="#cite_note-305">[298]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Television: <a href="/wiki/WCBS-TV" title="WCBS-TV">WCBS-2</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WNBC-TV" title="WNBC-TV">WNBC-4</a>, <a href="/wiki/WNYW" title="WNYW">WNYW-5</a>, <a href="/wiki/WABC-TV" title="WABC-TV">WABC-7</a>, <a href="/wiki/WWOR-TV" title="WWOR-TV">WWOR-9</a> <a href="/wiki/Secaucus,_New_Jersey" title="Secaucus, New Jersey">Secaucus</a>, <a href="/wiki/WPIX" title="WPIX">WPIX-11</a>, <a href="/wiki/WNET" title="WNET">WNET-13</a> <a href="/wiki/Newark,_New_Jersey" title="Newark, New Jersey">Newark</a>, <a href="/wiki/WNYE-TV" title="WNYE-TV">WNYE-25</a>, <a href="/wiki/WPXN-TV" title="WPXN-TV">WPXN-31</a>, <a href="/wiki/WXTV-DT" title="WXTV-DT">WXTV-41</a> <a href="/wiki/Paterson,_New_Jersey" title="Paterson, New Jersey">Paterson</a>, <a href="/wiki/WNJU" title="WNJU">WNJU-47</a> <a href="/wiki/Linden,_New_Jersey" title="Linden, New Jersey">Linden</a> and <a href="/wiki/WFUT-DT" title="WFUT-DT">WFUT-68</a> <a href="/wiki/Newark,_New_Jersey" title="Newark, New Jersey">Newark</a></li>
