<li>FM: <a href="/wiki/WBMP_(FM)" title="WBMP (FM)">WBMP-92.3</a>, <a href="/wiki/WPAT-FM" title="WPAT-FM">WPAT-93.1</a> <a href="/wiki/Paterson,_New_Jersey" title="Paterson, New Jersey">Paterson</a>, <a href="/wiki/WNYC-FM" title="WNYC-FM">WNYC-93.9</a>, <a href="/wiki/WPLJ" title="WPLJ">WPLJ-95.5</a>, <a href="/wiki/WXNY-FM" title="WXNY-FM">WXNY-96.3</a>, <a href="/wiki/WQHT" title="WQHT">WQHT-97.1</a>, <a href="/wiki/WSKQ-FM" title="WSKQ-FM">WSKQ-97.9</a>, <a href="/wiki/WEPN-FM" title="WEPN-FM">WEPN-98.7</a>, <a href="/wiki/WBAI" title="WBAI">WBAI-99.5</a>, <a href="/wiki/WHTZ" title="WHTZ">WHTZ-100.3</a> <a href="/wiki/Newark,_New_Jersey" title="Newark, New Jersey">Newark</a>, <a href="/wiki/WCBS-FM" title="WCBS-FM">WCBS-101.1</a>, <a href="/wiki/WFAN-FM" title="WFAN-FM">WFAN-101.9</a>, <a href="/wiki/WNEW-FM" title="WNEW-FM">WNEW-FM-102.7</a>, <a href="/wiki/WKTU" title="WKTU">WKTU-103.5</a> <a href="/wiki/Lake_Success,_New_York" title="Lake Success, New York">Lake Success</a>, <a href="/wiki/WAXQ" title="WAXQ">WAXQ-104.3</a>, <a href="/wiki/WWPR-FM" title="WWPR-FM">WWPR-105.1</a>, <a href="/wiki/WQXR-FM" title="WQXR-FM">WQXR-105.9</a> <a href="/wiki/Newark,_New_Jersey" title="Newark, New Jersey">Newark</a>, <a href="/wiki/WLTW" title="WLTW">WLTW-106.7</a> and <a href="/wiki/WBLS" title="WBLS">WBLS-107.5</a></li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Observation_decks">Observation decks</span></h4>
<p>The 86th and 102nd floors contain observatories,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAl-Kodmany201771_306-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAl-Kodmany201771-306">[299]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lepik2008_280-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lepik2008-280">[274]</a></sup> which combined see an average of 4 million visitors per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt20111225_253-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyt20111225-253">[248]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-Million-Havent-Shown-2016_307-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-Million-Havent-Shown-2016-307">[300]</a></sup> Since opening, the observatories have been more popular than similar observatories at <a href="/wiki/30_Rockefeller_Plaza" title="30 Rockefeller Plaza">30 Rockefeller Plaza</a>, the Chrysler Building, the first One World Trade Center, or the <a href="/wiki/Woolworth_Building" title="Woolworth Building">Woolworth Building</a>, despite being more expensive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt20111225_253-2"><a href="#cite_note-nyt20111225-253">[248]</a></sup> Tourists must pay to visit the observation deck on the 86th floor and an additional amount for the 102nd floor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_308-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-308">[301]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 86th floor observatory contains both an enclosed section and a wide-open section.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFodor.27s2010154_309-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFodor.27s2010154-309">[302]</a></sup> The 102nd floor observatory is completely enclosed and much smaller than the first one. For several years until 2005, the 102nd floor observatory was closed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFodor.27s2010154_309-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFodor.27s2010154-309">[302]</a></sup> The observation decks were redesigned in mid-1979.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oth19791128_222-1"><a href="#cite_note-oth19791128-222">[217]</a></sup></p>
<p>The lines to enter the observation decks, according to <a href="/wiki/Cond%C3%A9_Nast_Traveler" title="Condé Nast Traveler">Concierge.com</a>, are "as legendary as the building itself:" there are five of them: the sidewalk line, the lobby elevator line, the ticket purchase line, the second elevator line, and the line to get off the elevator and onto the observation deck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-310"><a href="#cite_note-310">[303]</a></sup> For an extra fee tourists can skip to the front of the line.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_308-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-308">[301]</a></sup> The Empire State Building garners significant revenue from ticket sales for its observation decks, making more money from ticket sales than it does from renting office space during some years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt20111225_253-3"><a href="#cite_note-nyt20111225-253">[248]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DaRo_23Apr2012_311-0"><a href="#cite_note-DaRo_23Apr2012-311">[304]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:auto; max-width:2008px;">
<div class="thumbinner" style="">
<div class="overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Skyline-New-York-City.jpg" title="A 360° panoramic view of New York City from the 86th-floor observation deck of the Empire State Building, spring 2005. <div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Skyline-New-York-City.jpg" title="File:Skyline-New-York-City.jpg"></a></div>
A 360° panoramic view of New York City from the 86th-floor observation deck of the Empire State Building, spring 2005. East River is to the left, Hudson River to the right, south is near center.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="New_York_Skyride">New York Skyride</span></h4>
<p>The Empire State Building also has a <a href="/wiki/Motion_simulator" title="Motion simulator">motion simulator</a> attraction located on the 2nd floor, opened in early 1994.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-312"><a href="#cite_note-312">[305]</a></sup> It was built as a complement to the observation deck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyr20011105_313-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyr20011105-313">[306]</a></sup> The cinematic presentation lasts approximately 25 minutes, while the simulation is about eight minutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-314"><a href="#cite_note-314">[307]</a></sup> As of October 2017<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Empire_State_Building&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, tickets are $42 for adults, $32 for children, and $36 for senior citizens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-315"><a href="#cite_note-315">[308]</a></sup></p>
<p>The ride has had two incarnations. The original version, which ran from 1994 until around 2002, featured <a href="/wiki/James_Doohan" title="James Doohan">James Doohan</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Trek:_The_Original_Series" title="Star Trek: The Original Series">Star Trek's</a></i> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Montgomery_Scott" title="Montgomery Scott">Scotty</a>, as the airplane's pilot, who humorously tried to keep the flight under control during a storm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-316"><a href="#cite_note-316">[309]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-317"><a href="#cite_note-317">[310]</a></sup> After the World Trade Center terrorist attacks in September 11, 2001, the ride was closed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyr20011105_313-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyr20011105-313">[306]</a></sup> An updated version debuted in mid-2002 with actor <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Bacon" title="Kevin Bacon">Kevin Bacon</a> as the pilot, with the new flight also going haywire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fodors2012_318-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fodors2012-318">[311]</a></sup> This new version served a more informative goal, as opposed to the old version's main purpose of entertainment, and contained details about the 9/11 attacks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-319"><a href="#cite_note-319">[312]</a></sup> The simulator has received mixed reviews, with assessments of the ride ranging from "great" to "satisfactory" to "corny".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-320"><a href="#cite_note-320">[313]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lights">Lights</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Empire_State_Building_Red_and_Green.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="2448" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Empire_State_Building_Red_and_Green.JPG/220px-Empire_State_Building_Red_and_Green.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Empire_State_Building_Red_and_Green.JPG/330px-Empire_State_Building_Red_and_Green.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Empire_State_Building_Red_and_Green.JPG/440px-Empire_State_Building_Red_and_Green.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Empire_State_Building_Red_and_Green.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Lighting at the top of the Empire State Building</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The building was originally equipped with white <a href="/wiki/Searchlight" title="Searchlight">searchlights</a> atop the tower. They saw their first use in November 1932 when they lit up to signal Roosevelt's victory over Hoover in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1932" title="United States presidential election, 1932">presidential election of that year</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Melina_2010_321-0"><a href="#cite_note-Melina_2010-321">[314]</a></sup> They were swapped for four "Freedom Lights" in 1956.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Melina_2010_321-1"><a href="#cite_note-Melina_2010-321">[314]</a></sup> In February 1964, flood lights were added on the 72nd floor<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014353_205-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014353-205">[200]</a></sup> to illuminate the top of the building at night so that the building could be seen from the World's Fair later that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19640121_206-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19640121-206">[201]</a></sup> The lights were shut off from November 1973 to July 1974 because of the <a href="/wiki/1970s_energy_crisis" title="1970s energy crisis">severe energy crisis</a> at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_51-3"><a href="#cite_note-history-51">[47]</a></sup> New red, white, and blue lights were installed in 1976 in time for the country's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bicentennial_of_the_United_States" title="Bicentennial of the United States">bicentennial</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_51-4"><a href="#cite_note-history-51">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBermanMuseum_of_New_York_City2003108_322-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBermanMuseum_of_New_York_City2003108-322">[315]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since 1976 the spire has been lit in colors chosen to match seasonal events and holidays, with organizations able to make requests through the building's website.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-323"><a href="#cite_note-323">[316]</a></sup> The building will also be lit in the colors of New York's sports teams on the nights they have home games (orange, blue and white for the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Knicks" title="New York Knicks">New York Knicks</a>, red, white and blue for the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Rangers" title="New York Rangers">New York Rangers</a>, and so on).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-324"><a href="#cite_note-324">[317]</a></sup> It was twice lit in scarlet to support New Jersey's <a href="/wiki/Rutgers_University" title="Rutgers University">Rutgers University</a>: once for a <a href="/wiki/American_football" title="American football">football</a> game against the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Louisville" title="University of Louisville">University of Louisville</a> on November 9, 2006, and again on April 3, 2007 when the women's basketball team played in the national championship game.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-325"><a href="#cite_note-325">[318]</a></sup></p>
<p>There have also been special occasions where the lights are modified from the usual schedule. After the eightieth birthday and subsequent death of <a href="/wiki/Frank_Sinatra" title="Frank Sinatra">Frank Sinatra</a> in 1998, for example, the building was bathed in blue light to represent the singer's nickname "Ol' Blue Eyes".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-326"><a href="#cite_note-326">[319]</a></sup> After actress <a href="/wiki/Fay_Wray" title="Fay Wray">Fay Wray</a> died in September 2004, who starred in <i><a href="/wiki/King_Kong_(1933_film)" title="King Kong (1933 film)">King Kong</a></i>, the building stood in complete darkness for 15 minutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-327"><a href="#cite_note-327">[320]</a></sup> The floodlights bathed the building in red, white, and blue for several months after the destruction of the World Trade Center, then reverted to the standard schedule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lightingSchedule_328-0"><a href="#cite_note-lightingSchedule-328">[321]</a></sup> On June 4, 2002, the Empire State Building donned purple and gold (the royal colors of <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Elizabeth II</a>), in thanks for the United Kingdom playing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Star_Spangled_Banner" title="Star Spangled Banner">Star Spangled Banner</a> during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guard_Mounting" title="Guard Mounting">Changing of the Guard</a> at <a href="/wiki/Buckingham_Palace" title="Buckingham Palace">Buckingham Palace</a> on September 12, 2001 (a show of support after the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-329"><a href="#cite_note-329">[322]</a></sup> On January 13, 2012, the building was lit in red, orange, and yellow to honor the 60th anniversary of <a href="/wiki/NBC_News" title="NBC News">NBC</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Today_(U.S._TV_program)" title="Today (U.S. TV program)"><i>The Today Show</i></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-330"><a href="#cite_note-330">[323]</a></sup> From June 1 to 3, 2012, the building was lit in blue and white, the colors of the <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Israel" title="Flag of Israel">Israeli flag</a>, in honor of the 49th annual <a href="/wiki/Celebrate_Israel_Parade" title="Celebrate Israel Parade">Celebrate Israel Parade</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-331"><a href="#cite_note-331">[324]</a></sup></p>
<p>During 2012, the building's four hundred <a href="/wiki/Metal-halide_lamp" title="Metal-halide lamp">metal halide</a> lamps and floodlights were replaced with 1,200 <a href="/wiki/Light-emitting_diode" title="Light-emitting diode">LED</a> fixtures, increasing the available colors from nine to over 16 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-332"><a href="#cite_note-332">[325]</a></sup> The computer-controlled system allows the building to be illuminated in ways that were unable to be done previously with plastic gels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Santora_2013_333-0"><a href="#cite_note-Santora_2013-333">[326]</a></sup> For instance, on November 6, 2012, <a href="/wiki/CNN" title="CNN">CNN</a> used the top of the Empire State Building as a scoreboard for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2012_United_States_presidential_election" title="2012 United States presidential election">2012 United States presidential election</a>. When incumbent president <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> had reached the 270 electoral votes necessary to win re-election, the lights turned blue. Had Republican challenger <a href="/wiki/Mitt_Romney" title="Mitt Romney">Mitt Romney</a> won, the building would have been lit red.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-334"><a href="#cite_note-334">[327]</a></sup> Also, on November 26, 2012, the building had its first ever synchronized light show, using music from recording artist <a href="/wiki/Alicia_Keys" title="Alicia Keys">Alicia Keys</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-335"><a href="#cite_note-335">[328]</a></sup> The building's owners adhere to strict standards in using the lights; for instance, they would not use the lights to play advertisements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Santora_2013_333-1"><a href="#cite_note-Santora_2013-333">[326]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Height_records"><span id="Height_records_and_comparisons"></span>Height records</span></h3>

</div>
</div>
<p>The longest world record held by the Empire State Building was for the tallest skyscraper (to structural height), which it held for 42 years until it was surpassed by the <a href="/wiki/One_World_Trade_Center#Original_building" title="One World Trade Center">North Tower</a> of the World Trade Center in October 1970.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunlap_2012_238-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dunlap_2012-238">[233]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-336"><a href="#cite_note-336">[329]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CBS_News_2012_217-2"><a href="#cite_note-CBS_News_2012-217">[212]</a></sup> The Empire State Building was also the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_buildings_and_structures_in_the_world" title="List of tallest buildings and structures in the world">tallest man-made structure</a> in the world before it was surpassed by the <a href="/wiki/Griffin_Television_Tower_Oklahoma" title="Griffin Television Tower Oklahoma">Griffin Television Tower Oklahoma</a> (KWTV Mast) in 1954,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-337"><a href="#cite_note-337">[330]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_freestanding_structures" title="List of tallest freestanding structures">tallest freestanding structure in the world</a> until the completion of the <a href="/wiki/Ostankino_Tower" title="Ostankino Tower">Ostankino Tower</a> in 1967.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CBS_News_2012_217-3"><a href="#cite_note-CBS_News_2012-217">[212]</a></sup> An early-1970s proposal to dismantle the spire and replace it with an additional 11 floors, which would have brought the building's height to 1,494 feet (455 m) and made it once again the world's tallest at the time, was considered but ultimately rejected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-338"><a href="#cite_note-338">[331]</a></sup></p>
<p>With the destruction of the World Trade Center in the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a>, the Empire State Building again became the <a href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_buildings_in_New_York_City" title="List of tallest buildings in New York City">tallest building in New York City</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_buildings_in_the_world" title="List of tallest buildings in the world">second-tallest building in the Americas</a>, surpassed only by the <a href="/wiki/Willis_Tower" title="Willis Tower">Willis Tower</a> in Chicago. The Empire State Building remained the tallest building in New York until the new <a href="/wiki/One_World_Trade_Center" title="One World Trade Center">One World Trade Center</a> reached a greater height in April 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunlap_2012_238-2"><a href="#cite_note-Dunlap_2012-238">[233]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CBS_News_2012_217-4"><a href="#cite_note-CBS_News_2012-217">[212]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nydn20120430_239-1"><a href="#cite_note-nydn20120430-239">[234]</a></sup> As of July 2016<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Empire_State_Building&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, it is the third-tallest building in New York City after the One World Trade Center and <a href="/wiki/432_Park_Avenue" title="432 Park Avenue">432 Park Avenue</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-339"><a href="#cite_note-339">[332]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_buildings_in_the_United_States" title="List of tallest buildings in the United States">fifth-tallest completed skyscraper in the United States</a> behind the two other tallest buildings in New York City, as well as the Willis Tower and <a href="/wiki/Trump_International_Hotel_and_Tower_(Chicago)" title="Trump International Hotel and Tower (Chicago)">Trump International Hotel and Tower</a> in <a href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_buildings_in_Chicago" title="List of tallest buildings in Chicago">Chicago</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-340"><a href="#cite_note-340">[333]</a></sup> The Empire State Building is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_buildings_in_the_world" title="List of tallest buildings in the world">28th-tallest in the world</a> As of October 2017<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Empire_State_Building&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the tallest being <a href="/wiki/Burj_Khalifa" title="Burj Khalifa">Burj Khalifa</a> in Dubai.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-341"><a href="#cite_note-341">[334]</a></sup> It is also the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_freestanding_structures_in_the_world" title="List of tallest freestanding structures in the world">sixth-tallest freestanding structure in the Americas</a> behind the five tallest buildings and the <a href="/wiki/CN_Tower" title="CN Tower">CN Tower</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-342"><a href="#cite_note-342">[335]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Empire State Building Run-Up, a foot race from ground level to the 86th-floor observation deck, has been held annually since 1978. Its participants are referred to both as runners and as climbers, and are often <a href="/wiki/Tower_running" title="Tower running">tower running</a> enthusiasts. The race covers a vertical distance of 1,050 feet (320 m) and takes in 1,576 steps. The record time is 9 minutes and 33 seconds, achieved by Australian professional cyclist <a href="/wiki/Paul_Crake" title="Paul Crake">Paul Crake</a> in 2003, at a climbing rate of 6,593 ft (2,010 m) per hour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-343"><a href="#cite_note-343">[336]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-344"><a href="#cite_note-344">[337]</a></sup> ESRT began trading publicly on the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Stock_Exchange" title="New York Stock Exchange">New York Stock Exchange</a> on October 2, 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-345"><a href="#cite_note-345">[338]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:auto; max-width:808px;">
<div class="thumbinner" style="">
<div class="overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:New_York_Midtown_Skyline_at_night_-_Jan_2006_edit1.jpg" title="The Midtown skyline, as viewed from the observation deck at night in January 2006"><img alt="" data-file-height="1223" data-file-width="3496" height="280" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/New_York_Midtown_Skyline_at_night_-_Jan_2006_edit1.jpg/800px-New_York_Midtown_Skyline_at_night_-_Jan_2006_edit1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/New_York_Midtown_Skyline_at_night_-_Jan_2006_edit1.jpg/1200px-New_York_Midtown_Skyline_at_night_-_Jan_2006_edit1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/New_York_Midtown_Skyline_at_night_-_Jan_2006_edit1.jpg/1600px-New_York_Midtown_Skyline_at_night_-_Jan_2006_edit1.jpg 2x" width="800"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:New_York_Midtown_Skyline_at_night_-_Jan_2006_edit1.jpg" title="File:New York Midtown Skyline at night - Jan 2006 edit1.jpg"></a></div>
The Midtown skyline, as viewed from the observation deck at night in January 2006</div>
</div>
</div>

<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 25em; -webkit-column-width: 25em; column-width: 25em;">

</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Incidents">Incidents</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1945_plane_crash">1945 plane crash</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bomber_Crashed_into_Empire_State_Building_1945.jpg"><img alt="Aftermath of the 1945 B-35 plane crash" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="691" data-file-width="558" height="272" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Bomber_Crashed_into_Empire_State_Building_1945.jpg/220px-Bomber_Crashed_into_Empire_State_Building_1945.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Bomber_Crashed_into_Empire_State_Building_1945.jpg/330px-Bomber_Crashed_into_Empire_State_Building_1945.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Bomber_Crashed_into_Empire_State_Building_1945.jpg/440px-Bomber_Crashed_into_Empire_State_Building_1945.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bomber_Crashed_into_Empire_State_Building_1945.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Aftermath of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/B-25_Empire_State_Building_crash" title="B-25 Empire State Building crash">B-25 Empire State Building crash</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/B-25_Empire_State_Building_crash" title="B-25 Empire State Building crash">B-25 Empire State Building crash</a></div>
<p>At 9:40 am on July 28, 1945, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/B-25_Mitchell" title="B-25 Mitchell">B-25 Mitchell</a> bomber, piloted in thick fog by Lieutenant Colonel William Franklin Smith, Jr.,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-371"><a href="#cite_note-371">[364]</a></sup> crashed into the north side of the Empire State Building, between the 79th and 80th floors, where the offices of the <a href="/wiki/National_Catholic_Welfare_Council" title="National Catholic Welfare Council">National Catholic Welfare Council</a> were located.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJackson2010413.E2.80.93414_184-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJackson2010413.E2.80.93414-184">[179]</a></sup> One engine shot through the side opposite the impact and flew as far as the next block, where it landed on the roof of a nearby building, starting a fire that destroyed a penthouse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-372"><a href="#cite_note-372">[365]</a></sup> The other engine and part of the landing gear plummeted down an elevator shaft. The resulting fire was extinguished in 40 minutes. Fourteen people were killed in the incident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBermanMuseum_of_New_York_City200386_185-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBermanMuseum_of_New_York_City200386-185">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Observer-ESB-Challenged-1976_91-3"><a href="#cite_note-Observer-ESB-Challenged-1976-91">[87]</a></sup> Elevator operator Betty Lou Oliver survived a plunge of 75 stories inside an elevator, which still stands as the <a href="/wiki/Guinness_World_Records" title="Guinness World Records">Guinness World Record</a> for the longest survived elevator fall recorded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-373"><a href="#cite_note-373">[366]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite the damage and loss of life, the building was open for business on many floors on the following Monday, two days later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBermanMuseum_of_New_York_City200386_185-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBermanMuseum_of_New_York_City200386-185">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bde19450730_186-1"><a href="#cite_note-bde19450730-186">[181]</a></sup> The crash helped spur the passage of the long-pending <a href="/wiki/Federal_Tort_Claims_Act" title="Federal Tort Claims Act">Federal Tort Claims Act</a> of 1946, as well as the insertion of retroactive provisions into the law, allowing people to sue the government for the incident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-374"><a href="#cite_note-374">[367]</a></sup> Also as a result of the crash, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_Aeronautics_Administration_(United_States)" title="Civil Aeronautics Administration (United States)">Civil Aeronautics Administration</a> enacted strict regulations regarding flying over New York City, setting a minimum flying altitude of 2,500 feet (760 m) above sea level regardless of the weather condition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014330_375-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014330-375">[368]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBermanMuseum_of_New_York_City200386_185-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBermanMuseum_of_New_York_City200386-185">[180]</a></sup></p>
<p>A year later, on July 24, 1946, another aircraft narrowly missed striking the building. The unidentified twin-engine plane had scraped past the observation deck, scaring tourists there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-376"><a href="#cite_note-376">[369]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2000_elevator_plunge">2000 elevator plunge</span></h3>
<p>On January 24, 2000, an elevator in the building suddenly descended 40 stories after a cable that controlled the cabin's maximum speed was severed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-377"><a href="#cite_note-377">[370]</a></sup> The elevator fell from the 44th floor to the fourth floor, where a narrowed elevator shaft provided a second safety system. Despite the 40-floor fall, both of the passengers in the cabin at the time were only slightly injured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt20000125_378-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt20000125-378">[371]</a></sup> Since that elevator had no fourth-floor doors, the passengers were rescued by an adjacent elevator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-379"><a href="#cite_note-379">[372]</a></sup> After the fall, building inspectors reviewed all of the building's elevators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt20000125_378-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyt20000125-378">[371]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Suicide_attempts">Suicide attempts</span></h3>
<p>Because of the building's iconic status, it and other Midtown landmarks are popular locations for <a href="/wiki/Suicide" title="Suicide">suicide</a> attempts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-380"><a href="#cite_note-380">[373]</a></sup> More than 30 people have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jumper_(suicide)" title="Jumper (suicide)">attempted suicide</a> over the years by jumping from the upper parts of the building, with most attempts being successful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-381"><a href="#cite_note-381">[374]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-suicide1_382-0"><a href="#cite_note-suicide1-382">[375]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first suicide from the building occurred on April 7, 1831, before the tower was even completed, when a carpenter who had been laid-off went to the 58th floor and jumped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-383"><a href="#cite_note-383">[376]</a></sup> The first suicide after the building's opening occurred from the 86th floor observatory on February 1935, when 22-year-old Irma P. Eberhardt fell 1,029 feet (314 m) onto a <a href="/wiki/Marquee_(sign)" title="Marquee (sign)">marquee sign</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014242_384-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014242-384">[377]</a></sup> On December 16, 1943, William Lloyd Rambo, a 22-year-old ex-<a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">United States Navy</a> <a href="/wiki/Gunner%27s_mate" title="Gunner's mate">gunner's mate</a>, jumped to his death from the 86th floor, landing amidst Christmas shoppers on the street below.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-385"><a href="#cite_note-385">[378]</a></sup> In the early morning of September 27, 1946, <a href="/wiki/Posttraumatic_stress_disorder" title="Posttraumatic stress disorder">shell-shocked</a> 27 year-old Marine Douglas W. Brashear, Jr. jumped from the 76th-floor window of the Grant Advertising Agency after phoning a co-worker to tell her, "I know now this is the end." Police found his shoes 50 feet from his body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-386"><a href="#cite_note-386">[379]</a></sup></p>
<p>On May 1, 1947, 23-year-old <a href="/wiki/Evelyn_McHale" title="Evelyn McHale">Evelyn McHale</a> leapt to her death from the 86th floor observation deck and landed on a <a href="/wiki/Limousine" title="Limousine">limousine</a> parked at the curb. Photography student Robert Wiles took a photo of McHale's oddly intact corpse a few minutes after her death. The police found a suicide note among possessions that she left on the observation deck: "He is much better off without me.... I wouldn’t make a good wife for anybody". The photo ran in the edition of May 12, 1947 of <i><a href="/wiki/Life_(magazine)" title="Life (magazine)">Life</a></i> magazine<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-387"><a href="#cite_note-387">[380]</a></sup> and is often referred to as "The Most Beautiful Suicide". It was later used by visual artist <a href="/wiki/Andy_Warhol" title="Andy Warhol">Andy Warhol</a> in one of his prints entitled <i>Suicide (Fallen Body)</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-388"><a href="#cite_note-388">[381]</a></sup> A 7-foot (2.1 m) mesh fence was put up around the 86th floor terrace in December 1947 after five people tried to jump during a three-week span in October and November of that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19471205_389-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19471205-389">[382]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-suicide2_390-0"><a href="#cite_note-suicide2-390">[383]</a></sup> By then, sixteen people had died from suicide jumps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19471205_389-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19471205-389">[382]</a></sup></p>
<p>Only one person has jumped from the upper observatory. Frederick Eckert of <a href="/wiki/Astoria,_Queens" title="Astoria, Queens">Astoria</a> ran past a guard in the enclosed 102nd floor gallery on November 3, 1932 and jumped a gate leading to an outdoor catwalk intended for <a href="/wiki/Airship" title="Airship">dirigible</a> passengers. He landed and died on the roof of the 86th floor observation promenade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-391"><a href="#cite_note-391">[384]</a></sup></p>
<p>Two people have survived falls by not falling more than a floor. On December 2, 1979, Elvita Adams jumped from the 86th floor, only to be blown back onto a ledge on the 85th floor by a gust of wind and left with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Broken_hip" title="Broken hip">broken hip</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-392"><a href="#cite_note-392">[385]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-393"><a href="#cite_note-393">[386]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldman198063_394-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldman198063-394">[387]</a></sup> On April 25, 2013, a man fell from the 86th floor observation deck, but he landed alive with minor injuries on an 85th-floor ledge where security guards brought him inside and paramedics transferred him to a hospital for a psychiatric evaluation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-395"><a href="#cite_note-395">[388]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-396"><a href="#cite_note-396">[389]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shootings">Shootings</span></h3>
<p>Two fatal shootings have occurred in the direct vicinity of the Empire State Building. Abu Kamal, a 69-year-old Palestinian teacher, <a href="/wiki/1997_Empire_State_Building_shooting" title="1997 Empire State Building shooting">shot seven people</a> on the 86th floor observation deck during the afternoon of February 23, 1997. He killed one person and wounded six others, supposedly in response to events happening in <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_territories" title="Palestinian territories">Palestine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, before committing suicide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-397"><a href="#cite_note-397">[390]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the morning of August 24, 2012, 58-year-old Jeffrey T. Johnson <a href="/wiki/2012_Empire_State_Building_shooting" title="2012 Empire State Building shooting">shot and killed</a> a former co-worker on the building's Fifth Avenue sidewalk. He had been laid off from his job in 2011. Two police officers confronted the gunman, and he aimed his firearm at them. They responded by firing 16 shots, killing him but also wounding nine bystanders, most of whom were hit by fragments, although three took direct hits from bullets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNN_398-0"><a href="#cite_note-CNN-398">[391]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TWPFlr_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-TWPFlr-14">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Importance">Importance</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_icon">As icon</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Souvenirempirestate.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="1944" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Souvenirempirestate.JPG/170px-Souvenirempirestate.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Souvenirempirestate.JPG/255px-Souvenirempirestate.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Souvenirempirestate.JPG/340px-Souvenirempirestate.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Souvenirempirestate.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Souvenir of the building</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As the tallest building in the world and the first one to exceed 100 floors, the Empire State Building was immediately an icon of the city and of the entire country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young_Young_2007_p._144_145-5"><a href="#cite_note-Young_Young_2007_p._144-145">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199856_152-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillisFriedman199856-152">[148]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine called the building a way that "you know that you’re in New York City".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-399"><a href="#cite_note-399">[392]</a></sup> John Tauranac calls the tower "'the' twentieth-century New York building", even despite the existence of taller and more <a href="/wiki/Modern_architecture" title="Modern architecture">modernist</a> buildings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac201419_400-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac201419-400">[393]</a></sup> Early in the building's history, travel companies such as <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Short_Line_Motor_Coach_Service&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Short Line Motor Coach Service (page does not exist)">Short Line Motor Coach Service</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_York_Central_Railroad" title="New York Central Railroad">New York Central Railroad</a> used the building as an icon to symbolize the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac201420_401-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac201420-401">[394]</a></sup> After the construction of the first World Trade Center, architect <a href="/wiki/Paul_Goldberger" title="Paul Goldberger">Paul Goldberger</a> noted that the Empire State Building "is famous for being tall, but it is good enough to be famous for being good."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oth19791128_222-2"><a href="#cite_note-oth19791128-222">[217]</a></sup></p>
<p>As an icon of the United States, it is also very popular among Americans. In a 2007 survey, the American Institute of Architects found that the Empire State Building was "America's favorite building".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-402"><a href="#cite_note-402">[395]</a></sup> The building was originally a symbol of hope in a country devastated by the Depression, as well as a work of accomplishment by newer immigrants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young_Young_2007_p._144_145-6"><a href="#cite_note-Young_Young_2007_p._144-145">[141]</a></sup> Benjamin Flowers writes that the Empire State was "a building intended to celebrate a new America, built by men (both clients and construction workers) who were themselves new Americans."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlowers200172_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlowers200172-140">[136]</a></sup> Meanwhile, <a href="/wiki/BBC_News" title="BBC News">BBC News</a>' culture section referred to the building as an "icon of American design".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glancey_2013_346-1"><a href="#cite_note-Glancey_2013-346">[339]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Empire State Building has been hailed as an example of a "<a href="/wiki/Wonders_of_the_World" title="Wonders of the World">wonder of the world</a>" due to the massive effort expended during construction. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Star" title="The Washington Star">The Washington Star</a></i> listed it as part of one of the "seven wonders of the modern world" in 1931, while <i><a href="/wiki/Holiday_(magazine)" title="Holiday (magazine)">Holiday</a></i> magazine wrote in 1958 that the Empire State's height would be taller than the combined heights of the <a href="/wiki/Eiffel_Tower" title="Eiffel Tower">Eiffel Tower</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Great_Pyramid_of_Giza" title="Great Pyramid of Giza">Great Pyramid of Giza</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac201419_400-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac201419-400">[393]</a></sup> The American Society of Civil Engineers also declared the building "A Modern Civil Engineering Wonder of the United States" in 1958, and one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seven_Wonders_of_the_Modern_World" title="Seven Wonders of the Modern World">Seven Wonders of the Modern World</a> in 1994.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ASCE_Metropolitan_Section_200-1"><a href="#cite_note-ASCE_Metropolitan_Section-200">[195]</a></sup> Ron Miller, in a 2010 book, also described the Empire State Building as one of the "seven wonders of engineering".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-403"><a href="#cite_note-403">[396]</a></sup> It has often been called the <a href="/wiki/Eighth_Wonder_of_the_World" title="Eighth Wonder of the World">Eighth Wonder of the World</a> as well, an appellation that it has held since shortly after opening.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dupre_2013_79-3"><a href="#cite_note-Dupre_2013-79">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-404"><a href="#cite_note-404">[397]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Associated_Press_177-1"><a href="#cite_note-Associated_Press-177">[172]</a></sup> The panels installed in the lobby in 1963 reflected this, showing the seven original wonders alongside the Empire State Building.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arbus-bio_287-1"><a href="#cite_note-arbus-bio-287">[280]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Empire_State_Building_in_popular_culture" title="Empire State Building in popular culture">Empire State Building in popular culture</a></div>
<p>As an icon of New York City, the Empire State Building has been featured in dozens of films, books, TV shows, and video games. In his book about the Empire State Building, John Tauranac writes that the first documented appearance of the tower in popular culture was <i>Swiss Family Manhattan</i>, a 1932 children's story by <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Morley" title="Christopher Morley">Christopher Morley</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac201424_405-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac201424-405">[398]</a></sup> A year later, the film <i><a href="/wiki/King_Kong_(1933_film)" title="King Kong (1933 film)">King Kong</a></i> depicted <a href="/wiki/King_Kong" title="King Kong">Kong</a>, a large <a href="/wiki/Stop_motion" title="Stop motion">stop motion</a> ape, who climbs the Empire State Building.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac201425_180-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac201425-180">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200972_181-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200972-181">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFodor.27s2010153_279-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFodor.27s2010153-279">[273]</a></sup> That movie brought the Empire State Building into the popular imagination,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFodor.27s2010153_279-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFodor.27s2010153-279">[273]</a></sup> with subsequent movies such as <i><a href="/wiki/An_Affair_to_Remember" title="An Affair to Remember">An Affair to Remember</a></i> (1957), <i><a href="/wiki/Sleepless_in_Seattle" title="Sleepless in Seattle">Sleepless in Seattle</a></i> (1993), and <i><a href="/wiki/Independence_Day_(1996_film)" title="Independence Day (1996 film)">Independence Day</a></i> (1996) showing the building.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200973_406-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200973-406">[399]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-407"><a href="#cite_note-407">[400]</a></sup> The building has also been featured in other works, such as "<a href="/wiki/Daleks_in_Manhattan" title="Daleks in Manhattan">Daleks in Manhattan</a>", a 2007 episode of the TV series <i><a href="/wiki/Doctor_Who" title="Doctor Who">Doctor Who</a></i>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200973_406-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200973-406">[399]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Empire_(1964_film)" title="Empire (1964 film)">Empire</a></i>, an eight-hour black-and-white <a href="/wiki/Silent_film" title="Silent film">silent film</a> by <a href="/wiki/Andy_Warhol" title="Andy Warhol">Andy Warhol</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200973_406-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200973-406">[399]</a></sup> that was later added to the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress" title="Library of Congress">Library of Congress</a>'s <a href="/wiki/National_Film_Registry" title="National Film Registry">National Film Registry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-408"><a href="#cite_note-408">[401]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h3>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-EmpireZip-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-EmpireZip_1-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-EmpireZip_1-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">The Empire State Building is located within the 10001 zip code area,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[20]</a></sup> but 10118 has been assigned as the building's own zip code by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Postal_Service" title="United States Postal Service">United States Postal Service</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bi-facts_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-bi-facts-19">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amny-facts_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-amny-facts-20">[18]</a></sup> since 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt19800501_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt19800501-23">[21]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Floor_count-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Floor_count_10-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Floor_count_10-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Most sources state that there are 102 floors,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-skyscrapercenter_7-9"><a href="#cite_note-skyscrapercenter-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emporis_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-emporis-8">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-skypage_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-skypage-9">[8]</a></sup> but a few give a figure of 103 floors due to the presence of a balcony above the 102nd floor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TWPFlr_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-TWPFlr-14">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTimes-Perch-2011_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes-Perch-2011-15">[13]</a></sup> See <a href="#Opening_and_early_years">§ Opening and early years</a> and <a href="#Above_the_102nd_floor">§ Above the 102nd floor</a> for a detailed explanation.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-36">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Some sources say that this purchase was made for $2600.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timeline_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-timeline-34">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperling2003-p124_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sperling2003-p124-35">[33]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-41">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Some sources say the farm may have been purchased by Charles Lawton for $10,000 in 1825 and purchased by John Jacob Astor's son <a href="/wiki/William_Backhouse_Astor_Sr." title="William Backhouse Astor Sr.">William Backhouse Astor Sr.</a> in 1827,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timeline_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-timeline-34">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sperling2003-p124_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sperling2003-p124-35">[33]</a></sup> but others say John Jacob bought Thompson's farm directly in 1826.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[36]</a></sup> This conflates John's purchase of the entire parcel with William's subsequent purchase of a half-interest that included the current Empire State Building's land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199120_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorehouse_III199120-40">[37]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-101st-floor-172"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-101st-floor_172-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-101st-floor_172-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-101st-floor_172-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-101st-floor_172-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">The 101st floor was later renamed the 102nd floor and is 101 floors above ground. The former 102nd floor, now the 103rd floor, is now a balcony that is off-limits to the public, and is 102 floors above ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETauranac2014237_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETauranac2014237-171">[167]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-zoning-270"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-zoning_270-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As per the 1916 Zoning Act, the wall of any given tower that faces a street could only rise to a certain height, proportionate to the street's width, at which point the building had to be set back by a given proportion. This system of setbacks would continue until the tower reaches a floor level in which that level's floor area was 25% that of the ground level's area. After that 25% threshold was reached, the building could rise without restriction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangmead200981_70-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangmead200981-70">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaydenMunicipal_Art_Society20008_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaydenMunicipal_Art_Society20008-267">[262]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWillis199567_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillis199567-268">[263]</a></sup> This law was modified <a href="/wiki/590_Madison_Avenue#1961_Zoning_Resolution" title="590 Madison Avenue">in 1961</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaydenMunicipal_Art_Society200011.E2.80.9312_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaydenMunicipal_Art_Society200011.E2.80.9312-269">[264]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-285"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-285">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See <a href="#CITEREFLandmarks_Preservation_Commission1981">Landmarks Preservation Commission 1981</a>, PDF page 26, for a diagram of the lobby.</span></li>
</ol>
